reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import math

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

# Default ModelNew is PyTorch-only. run_and_check: use check_kernel=False with backend=cuda
# until the candidate file contains a real CUDA kernel (see kernel_static_checker).


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Graph-Fused VLA (GF-VLA) style **scene graph relation update** in one forward.

    Objects are nodes with vision-language aligned embeddings; directed pairs
    (i → j) carry pairwise conditioning (geometry, language grounding, or fused
    VLA features). This reference fuses:

      LayerNorm(nodes) → multi-head attention over objects with **pairwise
      relation MLP bias** on edges → residual write-back.

    Intended as a fusion target for navigation / manipulation stacks that maintain
    explicit scene graphs and update them from multimodal observations.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, num_heads: int):
        super().__init__()
        assert dim % num_heads == 0
        self.dim = dim
        self.pair_dim = pair_dim
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = dim // num_heads
        self.scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(self.head_dim)

        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)
        self.q_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.k_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.v_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.rel_mlp = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim),
            nn.SiLU(),
            nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads),
        )
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)

    def forward(
        self,
        nodes: torch.Tensor,
        pair_feats: torch.Tensor,
        edge_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            nodes: (B, N, D) object / region embeddings.
            pair_feats: (B, N, N, P) pairwise VLA or geometric conditioning.
            edge_mask: (B, N, N) float in {0, 1}; 1 keeps attention over column j.
        Returns:
            (B, N, D) updated node embeddings (residual around attention block).
        """
        B, N, D = nodes.shape
        _, N2, N3, P = pair_feats.shape
        assert N == N2 == N3 and P == self.pair_dim
        h = self.norm(nodes)

        q = self.q_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)
        k = self.k_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)
        v = self.v_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)

        logits = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)) * self.scale
        rel_bias = self.rel_mlp(pair_feats).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)
        logits = logits + rel_bias

        logits = logits.masked_fill(edge_mask.unsqueeze(1) < 0.5, float("-inf"))
        attn = F.softmax(logits, dim=-1)
        ctx = torch.matmul(attn, v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, N, D)
        return nodes + self.out_proj(ctx)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    """KernelBench candidate entry point; default delegates to ``Model``."""

    def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, num_heads: int):
        super().__init__()
        self._impl = Model(dim, pair_dim, num_heads)

    def forward(
        self,
        nodes: torch.Tensor,
        pair_feats: torch.Tensor,
        edge_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        return self._impl(nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask)


batch_size = 4
num_objects = 24
dim = 96
pair_dim = 16
num_heads = 4


def get_inputs():
    nodes = torch.randn(batch_size, num_objects, dim)
    pair_feats = torch.randn(batch_size, num_objects, num_objects, pair_dim)
    edge_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, num_objects, num_objects)
    return [nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim, pair_dim, num_heads]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward pass of the graph-fused attention module. Let's break down the computation: 1. Input: nodes (B, N, D), pair_feats (B, N, N, P), edge_mask (B, N, N) float {0,1} 2. LayerNorm over D dimension on nodes. 3. Linear projections: q_proj, k_proj, v_proj (no bias) -> (B, N, D) each. 4. Reshape to (B, num_heads, N, head_dim). 5. Compute logits = Q @ K^T * scale => (B, H, N, N). 6. rel_bias = rel_mlp(pair_feats): pair_feats (B,N,N,P) -> Linear(P,P) -> SiLU -> Linear(P,H) -> (B,N,N,H) -> permute(0,3,1,2) -> (B,H,N,N). 7. logits = logits + rel_bias. 8. logits.masked_fill(edge_mask.unsqueeze(1) < 0.5, -inf). 9. attn = softmax over dim=-1. 10. ctx = attn @ v -> (B,H,N,head_dim) -> transpose -> (B,N,H,head_dim) -> reshape (B,N,D). 11. out = nodes + out_proj(ctx) (Linear no bias). We need to implement this as a fused CUDA kernel using load_inline. The shapes: batch=4, N=24, D=96, P=16, H=4, head_dim=24. N=24 is small. B=4. So total operations are small-ish but we need to be fast. The naive reference does many separate PyTorch operations: LayerNorm, 3 linears, matmul, 2 small linears on pair_feats, add, mask, softmax, matmul, transpose, reshape, linear, add. We can fuse a lot of this into a single kernel to reduce memory bandwidth. Given N=24 is small, we can have each thread block handle one (b, h) or one (b). Since N is small, we can compute attention for all N x N in shared memory within a block. Given B=4, H=4, total 16 attention heads. Each head attends over N=24 tokens. That's small enough to keep Q,K,V,logits,attn in registers or shared memory. But D=96, P=16. The pair feats MLP is: Linear(P,P) + SiLU + Linear(P,H). We could precompute rel_bias in a separate kernel or fuse into the attention kernel. Since pair_feats is (B,N,N,P) = 4*24*24*16 = 36864 floats, small. The rel_bias is (B,H,N,N) = 4*4*24*24 = 9216 floats. We can compute rel_bias on the fly or precompute. Potential strategy: 1. Precompute Q,K,V by fusing LayerNorm + 3x Linear into a single kernel. Since D=96, we can have each thread handle one (b,n) and compute the 3*(D) outputs. Or use cuBLAS (but it's small). Actually with small sizes, custom kernel may be faster than separate ops due to kernel launch overhead and bandwidth reduction. 2. Compute rel_bias via a custom kernel: Linear1 + SiLU + Linear2 on pair_feats. Input shape (B,N,N,P). Since P=16 is very small, we can have each thread handle one (b,i,j) and compute the H=4 outputs. That's trivial. 3. Main attention kernel: for each (b, h), load Q[b,h,:,:], K[b,h,:,:], V[b,h,:,:], rel_bias[b,h,:,:], edge_mask[b,:,:]. Compute logits = Q*K^T*scale + rel_bias, apply mask (set to -inf where edge_mask < 0.5), compute softmax, compute ctx = attn * V, then do out_proj (Linear D->D) and add residual. But out_proj is a linear over D dimension: (B,N,D) -> Linear(D,D) no bias -> add to nodes. One fused kernel could do: compute Q,K,V (store in SMEM or global memory), then for each head, compute attention, accumulate ctx into a (B,N,D) buffer, then apply out_proj and add residual. But since N is small, we might launch one block per (b, n) for the attention? Actually attention is over N dimension. We could compute softmax and output for each query (b, n) across all heads. That is a block per query per head, or per query handling all heads. Let's compute arithmetic: - Q,K,V are size B*H*N*hd = 4*4*24*24 = 9216 each. - Pair feats MLP is small. - The attention matmul Q@K.T is O(B*H*N^2*hd) = 4*4*24*24*24 = 221184 FLOPs. - The attn@V is same. Total ~0.44 MFLOPs. Very small. - The 3 Linear projections: B*N*D*D = 4*24*96*96 = 884,736 each => ~2.6 MFLOPs. - out_proj: same ~0.88 MFLOPs. So total FLOPs is only a few million. The kernel will be heavily memory-bandwidth bound or latency-bound. Given that, fusing everything into one kernel is critical to reduce memory round trips. However, we need to be careful about register pressure and shared memory usage. Let's think about a fully fused kernel: one CUDA block handles one (b, n) or one (b). Let's consider handling one batch element b per block, and within the block compute everything for all n and all heads. Batch size B=4, so 4 blocks total. Each block would compute: - Load nodes[b, n, :] for all n (24*96=2304 floats). Compute LayerNorm mean and variance across D for each n. That's 24 reductions of length 96. Within a block of say 256 threads, we can assign threads to n and do warp reductions. - After LayerNorm, compute Q,K,V projections. Since D=96, we could store weight matrices in shared memory? W_q is 96x96 = 9216 floats = 36 KB. W_k, W_v also 36 KB each. Total 108 KB. Shared memory per SM is usually 100 KB (configurable up to ~164 KB). So we could fit all three in shared memory if we have enough. But we also need other buffers. Alternatively, we can load weights directly from global memory (L1 cached). Since the weights are small and reused across N=24 queries, caching should be effective. Actually, each weight matrix is 96*96=9216 floats. L1 cache on A100 is 192KB per SM? Actually L1 is 192KB shared between L1 and shared memory? On Ampere, L1 is 192KB per SM. So weight matrices should fit in L1. But to maximize performance, we might want to compute Q,K,V using matrix multiply: nodes_norm (N,D) @ W_q^T (D,D) = Q (N,D). Since N=24 is tiny, a cuBLAS call has high overhead. So a custom kernel where each thread computes one output element of Q (or multiple) is fine. Let's consider a block size of 256 threads. For each batch b: - Threads process n in parallel. For each n, a thread can compute a chunk of D outputs for Q, K, V. But we also need to compute the attention. After Q,K,V are computed (stored in registers or shared memory), we need to compute for each head h and query i, the attention over keys j. If we store Q and K in shared memory: Q is B*H*N*hd = 9216 floats = 36 KB. K same, V same. That's 108 KB. Within one block (one b), Q,K,V for that b are H*N*hd = 2304 floats each = 9 KB. So total 27 KB. That's very manageable in shared memory. Actually per batch element: Q (4*24*24=2304 floats), K same, V same. So 27 KB. Plus buffer for logits (H,N,N) = 4*24*24 = 2304 floats = 9 KB. Plus rel_bias same 9 KB. Plus edge_mask (N,N) = 576 floats = ~2.3 KB. Plus maybe ctx (N,D) = 2304 floats = 9 KB. Total ~56 KB. This fits easily in 96 KB shared memory. So a fused kernel per batch element b is very feasible. Block configuration: one block per b (4 blocks). Block size: maybe 256 or 512 threads. Since there is limited parallelism within a block (the workload is about 24*96*3 projections + attention etc.), 256 threads is plenty. Let's outline the kernel steps for a block handling batch b: Shared memory layout (per block): - smem_nodes: maybe not needed if we read from global directly. - smem_q: H * N * hd floats (27 KB) - smem_k: same (27 KB) - smem_v: same (27 KB) - smem_logits or smem_attn: H * N * N floats (9 KB) - can compute on the fly per query i. - smem_rel_bias: H * N * N floats (9 KB) - smem_edge_mask: N * N floats (2.3 KB) - smem_ctx: N * D floats (9 KB) or compute directly to output. - smem_out: N * D floats (9 KB) Total: ~56 KB. Actually we can reduce by computing per query i in registers and not storing full logits/attn. Alternatively, we can do the following: 1. Load pair_feats (N,N,P) into shared memory? That's 24*24*16 = 9216 floats = 36 KB. That's a bit large but okay. However, the MLP weights are small: W1 (P,P) = 16*16=256 floats, W2 (P,H)=16*4=64 floats. We can compute rel_bias on the fly: for each (i,j), compute MLP and store result in smem_rel_bias[h,i,j]. Given that pair_feats is only needed for the MLP, we could compute rel_bias in the same kernel or a separate kernel. Since it's small, computing on the fly per (i,j) is fine. That's maybe 10+ kernels. Each kernel has launch overhead (~5-10 us). So total overhead alone could be 50-100 us. Plus memory bandwidth. If we fuse into a single kernel, we avoid all that overhead. Let's design the kernel in detail. **Kernel signature:** ```cpp extern "C" __global__ void gf_vla_forward( const float* nodes, const float* pair_feats, const float* edge_mask, const float* norm_weight, const float* norm_bias, const float* q_weight, const float* k_weight, const float* v_weight, const float* rel_w1, const float* rel_b1, const float* rel_w2, const float* rel_b2, const float* out_weight, float* out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, float scale) ``` `nodes`: (B,N,D) `pair_feats`: (B,N,N,P) `edge_mask`: (B,N,N) `norm_weight`: (D,), `norm_bias`: (D,) `q_weight`: (D,D), column-major or row-major? PyTorch linear weight is (out,in) -> (D,D). In CUDA, we can treat as row-major where weight[d_out, d_in]. So `q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]`. `out_weight`: (D,D). Steps per block (blockIdx.x = b): 1. Load edge_mask for this b into smem_edge_mask (size N*N). Use all threads. 2. Compute LayerNorm on nodes[b] -> smem_nodes_norm. - For each n (0..N-1), compute mean over D, then variance, then normalize. - Use threads to parallelize across n and d. Since N is small, we can handle reductions with warp shuffles. - Actually easiest: each thread processes a few n's. For each n assigned to thread t: - Load D elements (stride maybe to coalesce? nodes[b, n, d] is contiguous in d. So threads in a warp reading same n but different d will be coalesced if d index is threadIdx.x. But we have many threads. - Let's have thread idx = tid. For each n, we can use a warp (32 threads) to compute mean and var. But we have 256 threads and N=24. So we can have 24 warps? No, 24 > 8 warps (256 threads). Alternative: process n sequentially, with all threads collaborating on one n at a time. - For n=0..N-1: - Threads 0..D-1 (or 0..255) load nodes[b,n,d] for d=tid and d=tid+blockDim.x etc. while d < D. - Compute sum using warp shuffle reduction. Since D=96, we can have 96 threads active for the sum. Then compute mean. - Compute variance similarly. - Then each thread writes normalized value * weight[d] + bias[d] to smem_nodes_norm[n,d]. - This requires __syncthreads() between n? Not necessarily if we process one n at a time with all threads, and use warp shuffles within the active threads. But we need to broadcast mean and var to all threads. If we have only 96 threads active, the rest can just wait. To simplify, we can just have each thread loop over n independently. Since D is only 96, each thread can process a few n's entirely: 24 n's across 256 threads => each thread does ~0-1 n. Some threads will be idle. But we need to write to shared memory. Let's do: `int n = threadIdx.x;` if (n < N) { // compute mean and var for this n by looping over d } __syncthreads(); This uses only 24 threads. That's fine; the rest are idle. This avoids complex reductions. Each thread reads 96 floats, computes mean (96 adds), var (96 adds/muls), writes 96 floats. Very fast. 3. Projections Q,K,V: For each output position (n, d_out), compute dot product of smem_nodes_norm[n, :] with weight[d_out, :]. There are N*D = 2304 outputs per projection. We have 256 threads. Each thread can compute a few outputs. `int idx = threadIdx.x;` `int total = N * D;` `for (int linear = idx; linear < total; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float sum = 0; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) sum += smem_nodes_norm[n*D + d_in] * q_weight[d_out*D + d_in]; smem_q[linear] = sum; }` Similarly for K and V. This reads smem_nodes_norm (24*96=2304 floats) repeatedly. Since D=96 is small, the inner loop is short. The weights are read from global memory. Not ideal for memory reuse but total memory is tiny, so L1 caching should handle it. We can do the three projections with a single loop over linear idx and do three dot products simultaneously to amortize the load of nodes_norm. That is, each thread loads `float x = smem_nodes_norm[n*D + d_in];` and accumulates three sums. That's better. Then __syncthreads(). 4. Compute rel_bias: For each (i,j) pair, compute MLP: pair_feats[b,i,j,:] (P=16) -> Linear1 (w1: P*P, b1: P) -> SiLU -> Linear2 (w2: H*P, b2: H). Output: rel_bias[h,i,j]. Total outputs: H*N*N = 2304. Again assign `int idx = threadIdx.x; for (int linear = idx; linear < H*N*N; linear += blockDim.x) { ... }` Each thread handles a few (h,i,j). So we can compute the intermediate (P) once per (i,j), then the H outputs. Total pairs = N*N = 576. So we can have each thread handle a few (i,j) pairs and compute all H outputs for that pair. `int pair_idx = threadIdx.x;` `for (int p = pair_idx; p < N*N; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; // load pair_feats[b,i,j,d] for d=0..P-1 // compute hidden = SiLU(pair_feats @ w1^T + b1) // compute out_h = hidden @ w2^T + b2 // write to smem_rel_bias[h*N*N + i*N + j] }` Here w1 is (P,P). Let's outline per thread: ``` for (int linear = threadIdx.x; linear < H*N; linear += blockDim.x) { int h = linear / N; int i = linear % N; int base = (h * N + i) * N; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, smem_logits[base + j]); } float sum = 0.f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_logits[base + j] - max_val); smem_logits[base + j] = e; // overwrite with exp sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { float acc = 0.f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_logits[base + j] * inv_sum * smem_v[((h*N)+j)*hd + d]; } smem_ctx[((h*N)+i)*hd + d] = acc; } } ``` This is safe because each thread only accesses `base + j` that it wrote. Phase 2: Compute rel_bias -> write to smem_rel_bias. Sync. Phase 3: Compute logits = dot(Q,K)*scale + rel_bias, mask -> write to smem_logits. Sync. Phase 4: Softmax + ctx -> write to smem_ctx. Sync. Phase 5: out_proj + residual -> write to global out. This is clean and correct. Let's refine each phase with thread mapping. **Phase 1: LayerNorm + Projections** We need to compute smem_nodes_norm (N*D). As discussed, each thread can handle one n if threadIdx.x < N. ``` int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid < N) { int n = tid; // compute mean float sum = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) sum += nodes[((b*N)+n)*D + d]; float mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float diff = nodes[((b*N)+n)*D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float val = (nodes[((b*N)+n)*D + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d]; smem_nodes_norm[n*D + d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); ``` Only 24 threads active. That's fine. Then compute Q,K,V. Since all threads are available, we parallelize over (n,d_out): ``` int total_nd = N * D; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_nd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float sum_q = 0, sum_k = 0, sum_v = 0; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = smem_nodes_norm[n*D + d_in]; sum_q += x * q_weight[d_out*D + d_in]; sum_k += x * k_weight[d_out*D + d_in]; sum_v += x * v_weight[d_out*D + d_in]; } smem_q[linear] = sum_q; smem_k[linear] = sum_k; smem_v[linear] = sum_v; } __syncthreads(); ``` Here `q_weight` is (D,D). In PyTorch, `q_proj.weight` shape is (D,D). When accessed as `q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]`, this corresponds to row-major. PyTorch stores weight as (out_features, in_features), so `weight[out, in]`. So linear is `y[out] = sum_in x[in] * weight[out, in]`. That matches. **Phase 2: rel_bias** ``` int total_pairs = N * N; for (int p = tid; p < total_pairs; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; int feat_base = ((b * N + i) * N + j) * P; // first linear float hidden[16]; // P max 16 for (int p_out = 0; p_out < P; ++p_out) { float sum = rel_b1[p_out]; for (int p_in = 0; p_in < P; ++p_in) { sum += pair_feats[feat_base + p_in] * rel_w1[p_out * P + p_in]; } hidden[p_out] = sum; } // SiLU for (int p_out = 0; p_out < P; ++p_out) { float x = hidden[p_out]; hidden[p_out] = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))); // sigmoid, then multiply // Actually SiLU(x) = x * sigmoid(x) // so hidden[p_out] = x * sigmoid(x); } // second linear for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { float sum = rel_b2[h]; for (int p_in = 0; p_in < P; ++p_in) { sum += hidden[p_in] * rel_w2[h * P + p_in]; } smem_rel_bias[((h * N) + i) * N + j] = sum; } } __syncthreads(); ``` We used a local array `hidden[16]`. Since P=16, this is small. The compiler might put it in registers. To be safe, we can unroll or declare as `float h0, h1, ...`. But an array of 16 floats is okay. However, if P is not a compile-time constant? The problem says P=16, but we can keep it dynamic or template. Using dynamic loops is fine. Wait, we need `smem_rel_bias` as shared memory. We must allocate it. **Phase 3: logits** ``` int total_qk = H * N * N; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_qk; linear += blockDim.x) { int h = linear / (N * N); int rem = linear % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; float dot = 0; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_q[((h*N)+i)*hd + d] * smem_k[((h*N)+j)*hd + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel_bias[linear]; if (smem_edge_mask[i*N + j] < 0.5f) logit = -INFINITY; smem_logits[linear] = logit; } __syncthreads(); ``` Need `smem_edge_mask`. We can load it in phase 0. ``` for (int p = tid; p < N*N; p += blockDim.x) { smem_edge_mask[p] = edge_mask[b*N*N + p]; } __syncthreads(); ``` Put this at the start. **Phase 4: Softmax + ctx** ``` int total_q = H * N; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_q; linear += blockDim.x) { int h = linear / N; int i = linear % N; int base = (h * N + i) * N; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float val = smem_logits[base + j]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } float sum = 0.f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_logits[base + j] - max_val); smem_logits[base + j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { float acc = 0.f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_logits[base + j] * inv_sum * smem_v[((h*N)+j)*hd + d]; } smem_ctx[((h*N)+i)*hd + d] = acc; } } __syncthreads(); ``` Note: We overwrite smem_logits with exp values. This is okay. **Phase 5: out_proj + residual** `out_proj` maps ctx (N,D) -> (N,D). We can parallelize over (n,d_out): ``` for (int linear = tid; linear < total_nd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float sum = 0; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { sum += smem_ctx[n*D + d_in] * out_weight[d_out*D + d_in]; } float out_val = nodes[((b*N)+n)*D + d_out] + sum; out[((b*N)+n)*D + d_out] = out_val; } ``` Done. Now, we need to write the C++ code and the Python wrapper. **Python side:** ModelNew needs to: - Accept `dim`, `pair_dim`, `num_heads`. - Create parameters (nn.Linear, nn.LayerNorm, etc.) to match the reference, so that weights are loaded correctly. Or we can internally use the PyTorch modules to hold weights and then extract their pointers. That's easiest: we can create `self.norm`, `self.q_proj`, etc., and then in forward, call the CUDA kernel passing `.weight.data_ptr()` etc. Let's check: `nn.Linear(D, D, bias=False).weight` shape is (D,D). `nn.LayerNorm(D).weight` shape (D,), `.bias` shape (D,). `nn.Sequential(nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim), nn.SiLU(), nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads))`. So `self.rel_mlp[0].weight` shape (pair_dim, pair_dim). `.bias` shape (pair_dim,). `self.rel_mlp[2].weight` shape (num_heads, pair_dim). `.bias` shape (num_heads,). `self.out_proj.weight` shape (D,D). In `forward(self, nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask)`, we need to call the CUDA kernel. We need to ensure contiguous memory and float32. The kernel code has to be compiled via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. **Memory layout assumptions:** PyTorch tensors are row-major (last dimension contiguous). So: - nodes: (B, N, D) contiguous in D. - pair_feats: (B, N, N, P) contiguous in P. - edge_mask: (B, N, N) contiguous in last dim (N). Actually it's (B,N,N). The last two dims are N,N. Since it's 3D, it's contiguous in the last dimension, so `edge_mask[b,i,j]` is at `((b * N) + i) * N + j`. - Linear weights: (out,in) stored row-major? Actually in PyTorch, linear weight is stored as (out_features, in_features), so `weight[out, in]` is contiguous in `in`. So `weight[d_out * D + d_in]`. **Static check:** We shouldn't use try/except fallback to reference. Our ModelNew will always call the CUDA kernel. We need to make sure the kernel handles non-CUDA inputs gracefully or we just call `.cuda()`. The inputs in the benchmark are likely on CUDA already, but we can check `nodes.is_cuda`. However, the benchmark may pass CPU tensors? Usually in KernelBench, inputs are on the default device, which might be CUDA. The default ModelNew uses Model which works on CPU or CUDA. The task says "faster than the PyTorch reference", presumably on GPU. The benchmark likely runs on CUDA. To be safe, we can call `nodes = nodes.contiguous().float(); pair_feats = pair_feats.contiguous().float(); edge_mask = edge_mask.contiguous().float();` and maybe ensure they are on the same device. The reference code doesn't explicitly call `.cuda()`. However, the check script might. Usually KernelBench runs on CUDA. Let's write the wrapper. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> #include <math.h> extern "C" __global__ void gf_vla_forward_kernel( const float* nodes, const float* pair_feats, const float* edge_mask, const float* norm_weight, const float* norm_bias, const float* q_weight, const float* k_weight, const float* v_weight, const float* rel_w1, const float* rel_b1, const float* rel_w2, const float* rel_b2, const float* out_weight, float* out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, float scale) { // Shared memory ... } torch::Tensor gf_vla_forward( torch::Tensor nodes, torch::Tensor pair_feats, torch::Tensor edge_mask, torch::Tensor norm_weight, torch::Tensor norm_bias, torch::Tensor q_weight, torch::Tensor k_weight, torch::Tensor v_weight, torch::Tensor rel_w1, torch::Tensor rel_b1, torch::Tensor rel_w2, torch::Tensor rel_b2, torch::Tensor out_weight) { // check inputs // launch kernel return out; } """ cpp_source = "torch::Tensor gf_vla_forward(...);" # Actually load_inline signature: # load_inline(name, cpp_sources, cuda_sources, functions, ...) ``` Note: We need to write the host function in `cpp_sources` and kernel in `cuda_sources`. `load_inline` auto-generates pybind11 bindings for functions listed in `functions`. Let's design the host function `gf_vla_forward` in `cpp_sources` and the kernel in `cuda_sources`. The host function extracts pointers and dimensions, allocates output tensor, and launches the kernel. Important: We must include `ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h` for `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()`. **Detailed Kernel Code:** Let's write the kernel carefully. ```cuda extern "C" __global__ void gf_vla_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ q_weight, const float* __restrict__ k_weight, const float* __restrict__ v_weight, const float* __restrict__ rel_w1, const float* __restrict__ rel_b1, const float* __restrict__ rel_w2, const float* __restrict__ rel_b2, const float* __restrict__ out_weight, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, float scale) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int hd = D / H; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // layout: nodes_norm, q, k, v, rel_bias, logits, edge_mask, ctx float* smem_nodes_norm = smem; // N*D float* smem_q = smem_nodes_norm + N * D; // N*D float* smem_k = smem_q + N * D; // N*D float* smem_v = smem_k + N * D; // N*D float* smem_rel_bias = smem_v + N * D; // H*N*N float* smem_logits = smem_rel_bias + H * N * N; // H*N*N float* smem_edge_mask = smem_logits + H * N * N; // N*N float* smem_ctx = smem_edge_mask + N * N; // N*D // total size dynamic // Load edge mask for (int p = tid; p < N * N; p += blockDim.x) { smem_edge_mask[p] = edge_mask[b * N * N + p]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm if (tid < N) { int n = tid; float sum = 0.0f; int base = ((b * N) + n) * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { sum += nodes[base + d]; } float mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float diff = nodes[base + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float val = (nodes[base + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d]; smem_nodes_norm[n * D + d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); // Projections Q, K, V int total_nd = N * D; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_nd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float sum_q = 0.0f; float sum_k = 0.0f; float sum_v = 0.0f; float* x_ptr = smem_nodes_norm + n * D; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = x_ptr[d_in]; sum_q += x * q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; sum_k += x * k_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; sum_v += x * v_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; } smem_q[linear] = sum_q; smem_k[linear] = sum_k; smem_v[linear] = sum_v; } __syncthreads(); // Pairwise MLP -> rel_bias int total_pairs = N * N; for (int p = tid; p < total_pairs; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; int feat_base = ((b * N + i) * N + j) * P; // hidden buffer on registers / local float hidden0, hidden1, hidden2, hidden3, hidden4, hidden5, hidden6, hidden7, hidden8, hidden9, hidden10, hidden11, hidden12, hidden13, hidden14, hidden15; // P=16, unrolled to avoid dynamic indexing? We can use array, but let's try manual. // Actually compiler handles small arrays fine. Let's use array for readability. float hidden[16]; for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) { float s = rel_b1[po]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) { s += pair_feats[feat_base + pi] * rel_w1[po * P + pi]; } hidden[po] = s; } for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) { float x = hidden[po]; float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); hidden[po] = x * sig; } for (int h_ = 0; h_ < H; ++h_) { float s = rel_b2[h_]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) { s += hidden[pi] * rel_w2[h_ * P + pi]; } smem_rel_bias[(h_ * N + i) * N + j] = s; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute logits int total_qk = H * N * N; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_qk; linear += blockDim.x) { int h_ = linear / (N * N); int rem = linear % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; float dot = 0.0f; int q_base = ((h_ * N) + i) * hd; int k_base = ((h_ * N) + j) * hd; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_q[q_base + d] * smem_k[k_base + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel_bias[linear]; if (smem_edge_mask[i * N + j] < 0.5f) { logit = -INFINITY; } smem_logits[linear] = logit; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax + attention * V int total_q = H * N; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_q; linear += blockDim.x) { int h_ = linear / N; int i = linear % N; int base = ((h_ * N) + i) * N; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float val = smem_logits[base + j]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_logits[base + j] - max_val); smem_logits[base + j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_logits[base + j] * inv_sum * smem_v[((h_ * N) + j) * hd + d]; } smem_ctx[((h_ * N) + i) * hd + d] = acc; } } __syncthreads(); // Out projection + residual for (int linear = tid; linear < total_nd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { sum += smem_ctx[n * D + d_in] * out_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; } int global_idx = ((b * N) + n) * D + d_out; out[global_idx] = nodes[global_idx] + sum; } } ``` Wait, `hidden[16]` array might cause local memory usage because `po` is dynamic. But with `-O3`, NVCC will likely keep it in registers because the size is small and the loops are compile-time constant (P is a runtime variable, but the compiler may not know it's 16). To be safe, we can replace P with a template parameter or a compile-time constant. Since the task says `pair_dim=16`, we could just assume it's constant, but the kernel receives `P` as argument. However, the compiler may not unroll loops over `P`. But an array of size 16 with indexing via loop variable might still be kept in registers, but NVCC sometimes spills arrays to local memory if the index is not known at compile time. To avoid any risk, we can process the MLP without an array: compute the first linear outputs one by one, apply SiLU, and accumulate into the second linear on the fly. Since the second linear needs all hidden values simultaneously, we either store them or compute them multiple times. Storing them is necessary. We can declare 16 scalars manually: ```cuda float h0 = rel_b1[0] + ...; ... ``` But that would require unrolling 16 times. We can do that with a small `#pragma unroll` if we make P a template parameter. Actually, we can create a templated kernel with `pair_dim` as a template parameter? But the host function calls the kernel with dynamic P. Since the problem states `pair_dim=16`, we can just hardcode P=16 in the kernel for this benchmark, but to be general, we can keep it dynamic and trust the compiler. Let me think about local memory usage. If `hidden[16]` goes to local memory, each thread will allocate 64 bytes. For 256 threads, that's 16KB local memory per block. Local memory is backed by L1 cache, so it's fast. It won't hurt performance much for this tiny kernel. So we can just use the array. But wait: will using local memory cause bank conflicts or slow down? Not really, local memory is private per thread, laid out across global memory (or cached in L1). Reads/writes are coalesced within a warp if accessed sequentially. Here each thread accesses its own array sequentially. So it will be cached well. It's fine. However, there is another issue: `expf(-x)` for SiLU and softmax. In softmax, `expf` is called 24 times per query. With 96 queries, that's 2304 `expf` calls per block. In 4 blocks, total 9216 `expf`s. That's trivial. **Host C++ Code:** ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> torch::Tensor gf_vla_forward( torch::Tensor nodes, torch::Tensor pair_feats, torch::Tensor edge_mask, torch::Tensor norm_weight, torch::Tensor norm_bias, torch::Tensor q_weight, torch::Tensor k_weight, torch::Tensor v_weight, torch::Tensor rel_w1, torch::Tensor rel_b1, torch::Tensor rel_w2, torch::Tensor rel_b2, torch::Tensor out_weight) { // Ensure contiguous and float32 nodes = nodes.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); pair_feats = pair_feats.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); edge_mask = edge_mask.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); // weights should already be float32 and contiguous on device int B = nodes.size(0); int N = nodes.size(1); int D = nodes.size(2); int P = pair_feats.size(3); int H = q_weight.size(0) / (D / ???) wait. q_weight is (D,D). We need num_heads. We can get H = q_weight.size(0) / (D / H) ... that's circular. Actually H can be inferred from rel_b2 size: rel_b2.size(0) is num_heads. Or out_weight is (D,D). Let's pass H as an argument from Python. But the function signature doesn't have H. We can compute H = rel_b2.size(0). ``` int H = rel_b2.size(0); ``` `float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(D / H);` auto out = torch::empty_like(nodes); // compute shared memory size int hd = D / H; size_t smem_size = 0; smem_size += N * D; // nodes_norm smem_size += N * D; // q smem_size += N * D; // k smem_size += N * D; // v smem_size += H * N * N; // rel_bias smem_size += H * N * N; // logits smem_size += N * N; // edge_mask smem_size += N * D; // ctx smem_size *= sizeof(float); auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); gf_vla_forward_kernel<<<B, 256, smem_size, stream>>>( nodes.data_ptr<float>(), pair_feats.data_ptr<float>(), edge_mask.data_ptr<float>(), norm_weight.data_ptr<float>(), norm_bias.data_ptr<float>(), q_weight.data_ptr<float>(), k_weight.data_ptr<float>(), v_weight.data_ptr<float>(), rel_w1.data_ptr<float>(), rel_b1.data_ptr<float>(), rel_w2.data_ptr<float>(), rel_b2.data_ptr<float>(), out_weight.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, D, P, H, scale); return out; } ``` Wait, `q_weight` is `nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False).weight`. Its shape is (dim, dim) = (96,96). `rel_b2` shape is (num_heads,) = (4,). So `int H = rel_b2.size(0);` works. Need to include `cmath` for `sqrtf`. Need to make sure kernel launch dimensions handle B. Since B=4, gridDim.x = B. BlockDim.x = 256. **Shared memory size calculation:** We need to compute the exact size on host and pass it. `size_t smem_size = (5 * N * D + 2 * H * N * N + N * N + N * D) * sizeof(float);` Let's recount: nodes_norm: N*D q: N*D k: N*D v: N*D rel_bias: H*N*N logits: H*N*N edge_mask: N*N ctx: N*D Total = 5*N*D + 2*H*N*N + N*N. The base pointer `smem` must be aligned appropriately. `float` is 4 bytes, so it's fine. **Potential issue:** `blockDim.x` is 256. In Phase 1, `if (tid < N)` only 24 threads active. That's okay. **Potential issue:** Numerical stability of `rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f)`. PyTorch LayerNorm uses `eps=1e-5` (default). So correct. **Potential issue:** The MLP `SiLU` implementation. `SiLU(x) = x * sigmoid(x)`. In PyTorch, `nn.SiLU()` uses `torch.nn.functional.silu`, which has a custom backward but forward is `x * torch.sigmoid(x)`. Our implementation is `x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)))`. That's `x / (1 + exp(-x))`. This is correct for `x * sigmoid(x)`? Let's check: `sigmoid(x) = 1 / (1 + exp(-x))`. `x * sigmoid(x) = x * 1 / (1 + exp(-x)) = x / (1 + exp(-x))`. Our code does exactly that. **Potential issue:** `edge_mask` is float. In PyTorch, `edge_mask.unsqueeze(1) < 0.5` compares with 0.5. Since values are 0 or 1, it's exact. We do `smem_edge_mask[i*N+j] < 0.5f`. Good. **Potential issue:** `out` should be `float*` and we write to `out[global_idx] = nodes[global_idx] + sum;`. Good. **Potential issue:** Tensor shapes and strides. We call `.contiguous()` on inputs. If N were larger, it may exceed shared memory. But the benchmark uses these sizes. However, the kernel should work for any sizes as long as shared memory fits. We don't need to handle dynamic sizing beyond that. But to be safe for generic sizes, we might want to use a dynamic shared memory size based on the actual N, D, H. We already do that. **Alternative optimization:** We can use `const int N_ = N;` but it's dynamic. Loops over N, D, hd are dynamic. The compiler won't unroll them. But let's first write and compile. **One more thing:** We need to be careful about `extern __shared__ float smem[];` and then indexing. Let's make sure to include `<math.h>`. **Thread divergence in softmax:** All threads in a warp that handle different (h,i) have the same control flow (loops over j and d). So no divergence. **Thread divergence in Phase 1:** Only first N threads active. The rest are idle. Since N=24, one warp will have 24 active and 8 inactive threads. Divergence across warps: only warp 0 has some inactive lanes. Minimal. **Potential numerical difference in softmax:** PyTorch computes `max` over the row, then subtracts, then `exp`, then `sum`, then divides. The order of operations in `max` and `sum` can differ slightly depending on loop order. But with N=24, the difference should be within tolerance (1e-4). Our manual dot product may have slightly different accumulation order. But again, with 24 elements, difference is tiny. Let's think about `transpose` and `permute`: The reference does: `q = self.q_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)` So q shape is (B,H,N,hd). `k.transpose(-2,-1)` is (B,H,hd,N). `matmul` gives (B,H,N,N). This is equivalent to our manual dot product: for each b,h,i,j, sum_d q[b,h,i,d]*k[b,h,j,d]. `rel_bias` is computed as `self.rel_mlp(pair_feats).permute(0,3,1,2)`. `pair_feats` is (B,N,N,P). `rel_mlp` outputs (B,N,N,H). `permute(0,3,1,2)` gives (B,H,N,N). Our `smem_rel_bias` is indexed `[(h * N + i) * N + j]`, which corresponds to (H,N,N). Good. `ctx` shape is (B,N,H,hd) after transpose, then reshape to (B,N,D). Our `smem_ctx` stores `((h*N)+i)*hd + d`, which is (H,N,hd). Then we reinterpret as `n*D + d_in` for the out_proj, which reads contiguously across D. This matches the reshape: `ctx.transpose(1,2).reshape(B,N,D)` means the memory layout of `ctx` after transpose is (B, H, N, hd). Wait: - `torch.matmul(attn, v)` gives (B, H, N, hd). - `.transpose(1, 2)` gives (B, N, H, hd). In memory, the dimensions are permuted. The elements at [b,n,h,d] are stored contiguously? Actually `.transpose()` only changes strides, not memory layout. Then `.reshape(B, N, D)` also changes strides but doesn't copy if contiguous. However, in PyTorch, `.transpose` followed by `.reshape` may require a copy if not contiguous. Let's check: `ctx` after `matmul` is contiguous as (B,H,N,hd). `.transpose(1,2)` gives strides that are not contiguous for (B,N,H,hd). Then `.reshape(B,N,D)` is equivalent to `.view(B,N,D)`. Since the total size is same, but the memory is not contiguous for (B,N,H,hd) because the inner dimension is hd, but after transpose(1,2), the dimensions are B, N, H, hd. Strides: stride for N is H*N*hd? Actually original shape (B,H,N,hd), strides (H*N*hd, N*hd, hd, 1). After transpose(1,2), shape (B,N,H,hd), strides (H*N*hd, hd, N*hd, 1). This is not contiguous because moving along H changes stride by N*hd, not 1. So `.reshape(B,N,D)` would require a copy? Actually `.reshape` returns a view if possible, else a copy. In this case, (B,N,H,hd) to (B,N,D) is a view because D=H*hd, and the last dimension stride is 1. The shape (B,N,H,hd) can be viewed as (B,N,D) because the innermost dimension is hd, and the next is H with stride N*hd. Wait, to view as (B,N,D), the last dimension D must have stride 1. In the transposed tensor, the last dimension is hd with stride 1. The next dimension is H with stride N*hd. If we merge H and hd into D, the stride for D would be 1. But the stride for H is N*hd. That means the elements along D are not contiguous? Actually D = H*hd. The memory layout is: for fixed b,n, the elements are ordered by h, then d: [h=0,d=0..hd-1], [h=1,d=0..hd-1], ... So they are contiguous in memory because the innermost dimension is hd (stride 1) and H has stride hd. Wait, original strides after transpose: (B,N,H,hd) -> strides (s_B, s_N, s_H, s_hd) = (H*N*hd, hd, N*hd, 1). For fixed b,n, the offset for (h,d) is n*hd + h*N*hd + d. As h increments by 1, offset increases by N*hd. That means the elements for consecutive h are separated by N*hd elements, not contiguous. So they are not contiguous. Merging H and hd into D would give a tensor where for fixed b,n, the D elements are at offsets [n*hd + h*N*hd + d]. This is NOT a contiguous block of length D. It's strided with stride hd within each head and large gap between heads. So `.reshape` would fail to be a view and would trigger a copy. PyTorch would copy the tensor to make it contiguous for (B,N,D). However, in the reference code, the copy happens implicitly in `out_proj(ctx)`, because `nn.Linear` expects the last dimension to be contiguous? Actually `nn.Linear` works on the last dimension: it applies `x @ W^T + b`. It views the input as (-1, in_features). It doesn't require full contiguity; it just needs the last stride to be 1. The stride for the last dimension (D) in the reshaped tensor would be 1 if we view correctly. But `reshape(B,N,D)` on a tensor with shape (B,N,H,hd) and strides (H*N*hd, hd, N*hd, 1) is actually a valid view because `D = H*hd`. Wait, is it valid? Let's check: In PyTorch, a reshape is a view if the tensor can be seen as a single contiguous chunk. The condition for merging dims d_i and d_{i+1} is that stride(d_{i+1}) == stride(d_i) * size(d_i). Here, we want to merge H and hd into D. stride(hd) = 1. stride(H) = N*hd. size(H) = H. stride(hd) != stride(H) * size(H) because 1 != N*hd * H (which is H*N*hd). So they cannot be merged into a single dimension in a view. Therefore `.reshape(B,N,D)` will create a copy (contiguous). Let's verify: In PyTorch: ```python import torch a = torch.randn(2,3,4,5) b = a.transpose(1,2) c = b.reshape(2,4,15) print(c.stride()) # likely (60, 15, 1) -> contiguous copy ``` Yes, it copies. So in the reference, `ctx` after transpose and reshape is contiguous (B,N,D). Our kernel writes `smem_ctx` as (H,N,hd) in shared memory, and then reads it as `n*D + d_in` for the out_proj. This is effectively doing the transpose+reshape manually, writing out the elements in contiguous order (B,N,D). Is the reference's `ctx` after reshape equal to concatenating heads along the last dimension? Let's check: `ctx` from matmul is (B,H,N,hd). Transpose to (B,N,H,hd). Reshape to (B,N,D). The resulting memory layout is contiguous: for each (b,n), the D elements are ordered by head then d: [head0_d0, head0_d1, ..., head0_d{hd-1}, head1_d0, ...]. Wait, if it's a copy to make contiguous, the copy will iterate over the transposed tensor in row-major order and write sequentially. The transposed tensor's row-major order for shape (B,N,H,hd) is: for each b, for each n, for each h, for each d. So the contiguous output is indeed head-major within each node. That is exactly the natural layout of `smem_ctx` if we stored it as `((h*N)+i)*hd + d` and then read it as `n*D + (h*hd + d)`. Wait, in our shared memory, we store `smem_ctx[((h*N)+i)*hd + d]`. If we then read for out_proj as `smem_ctx[n*D + d_in]`, what is the mapping? `n` is the query index `i`. `d_in` ranges from 0 to D-1. But `smem_ctx` is indexed by `(h*N+i)*hd + d`. For a fixed `i=n`, the elements for different `h` and `d` are at: h=0,d=0: (0*N+n)*hd + 0 = n*hd h=0,d=1: n*hd + 1 ... h=1,d=0: (1*N+n)*hd = N*hd + n*hd = n*hd + N*hd So the elements for different heads are spaced by `N*hd`, not contiguous! Our `smem_ctx` stores heads separated by N*hd. The reference's contiguous `ctx` after reshape stores heads contiguously. This is a critical bug! Our out_proj reads `smem_ctx[n*D + d_in]` which assumes contiguous layout per node, but our write in the attention phase writes `smem_ctx[((h*N)+i)*hd + d]` which is (H,N,hd) layout. So we have a mismatch. We need to ensure that `smem_ctx` is written in a layout that matches the contiguous (B,N,D) shape, or we need to transpose during the out_proj. Two options: 1. Write `smem_ctx` in transposed order: for each (h,i,d), write to `smem_ctx[i*D + h*hd + d]`. Then out_proj can read `smem_ctx[n*D + d_in]` correctly. 2. Keep `smem_ctx` as (H,N,hd) and change the out_proj to index correctly: `smem_ctx[((d_in / hd) * N + n) * hd + (d_in % hd)]`. Option 2 is easier: just change the indexing in out_proj. `int h_ = d_in / hd; int d = d_in % hd; float val = smem_ctx[((h_ * N) + n) * hd + d];` But we also need to make sure that the `ctx` buffer used for out_proj has the right data. In the attention phase, we wrote `smem_ctx[((h_ * N) + i) * hd + d] = acc;`. This is the (H,N,hd) layout. The reference's `ctx` after contiguous reshape is (B,N,D) with `ctx[b,n, h*hd + d]`. So option 2 is correct because it reads the transposed layout. Wait, why is the reference's `ctx` after reshape equal to `ctx[b,n, h*hd + d]`? Let's derive: - Before reshape: `ctx` shape (B,N,H,hd). The element at [b,n,h,d] in the transposed tensor gets mapped to position `( (b * N + n) * H + h ) * hd + d` in the contiguous copy. When reshaped to (B,N,D) where D=H*hd, the elements for fixed (b,n) are at positions `(b*N + n)*D + h*hd + d`. So yes, the contiguous representation is `out[b,n, h*hd + d] = ctx[b,n,h,d]`. - Our `smem_ctx[((h*N)+n)*hd + d]` stores the value at [h,n,d]. To read as the contiguous (B,N,D), we need to map `(h,n,d) -> n*D + h*hd + d`. So we should write `smem_ctx[n*D + h*hd + d] = acc;` during attention. Then out_proj can just read `smem_ctx[n*D + d_in]`. This might be better because out_proj is a matrix multiply over the D dimension, requiring sequential access. If we write `smem_ctx[n*D + h*hd + d]`, the inner loop of out_proj reads contiguously. So let's change the attention phase to write in transposed layout: ```cuda smem_ctx[i * D + h_ * hd + d] = acc; ``` And then out_proj: ```cuda float val = smem_ctx[n * D + d_in]; sum += val * out_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; ``` This is better for memory coalescing in out_proj (threads with consecutive `d_in` read contiguous addresses). Wait, in out_proj, each thread handles a fixed (n, d_out) and loops over d_in. The reads of `smem_ctx` are sequential by d_in, which is good for cache. The writes to `smem_ctx` in attention phase are done by different threads. In Phase 4, threads are parallelized over (h_, i). For a fixed i and varying h_, d, the writes to `smem_ctx[i*D + h_*hd + d]` are scattered across threads. But since we sync after writing, and only one block reads it sequentially, it's fine. Wait, is there a risk of bank conflicts when writing `smem_ctx[i*D + h_*hd + d]` in Phase 4? Each thread handles one (h_,i). For a given i, threads handling different h_ write to different regions of smem_ctx. They don't conflict because each thread is writing to different addresses (separated by hd). Within a warp, if threads handle different h_, they write to addresses separated by hd. If hd=24, then consecutive threads (by linear index) might have different h_? The linear index is `h_*N + i`. For fixed i, consecutive linear indices differ by 1 in h_? No, `linear = h_*N + i`. For fixed i, incrementing h_ by 1 increases linear by N=24. Threads in a warp have consecutive `threadIdx.x` (mod 32). Since total_q=96, the mapping is linear. Let's see: for threadIdx.x = 0..31, they correspond to linear = 0..31. Since N=24, linear 0 -> h=0,i=0; linear 1 -> h=0,i=1; ... linear 23 -> h=0,i=23; linear 24 -> h=1,i=0. So within a warp, threads handle different (h,i) pairs. When they write to `smem_ctx[i*D + h*hd + d]`, the addresses are `i*D + h*hd + d`. For different (h,i), these are far apart. No bank conflicts because each thread writes to its own unique address (or at least no two threads in the warp write to the same bank? Shared memory banks are 4 bytes. Address `a` goes to bank `a % 32`. For two different (h,i), `i*D + h*hd` can collide. For example, i=0,h=1 -> 1*96 = 96 -> bank 0. i=1,h=0 -> 1*96 = 96 -> bank 0. So threads with linear 0 (h=0,i=0) and linear 24 (h=1,i=0) might be in same warp? Warp 0: threads 0..23 -> h=0, i=0..23. Warp 1: threads 24..31 -> h=1, i=0..7. So threads in different warps. Within a warp, threads have same h, different i. Addresses: `i*D + h*hd + d`. For same h and d, consecutive i differ by D=96. 96 % 32 = 0. So all threads in the warp writing to `smem_ctx[i*D + h*hd + d]` for consecutive i will all map to the same shared memory bank! This is a severe bank conflict: 24-way bank conflict (or 32-way if blockDim.x is 32). Because D=96 is a multiple of 32. Wait, that is a problem. Let's analyze: In Phase 4, we have: ```cuda for (int linear = tid; linear < total_q; linear += blockDim.x) { int h_ = linear / N; int i = linear % N; ... for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { smem_ctx[i * D + h_ * hd + d] = acc; } } ``` If threads in a warp have same h_ and consecutive i (which they do, because linear increments by 1, and N=24, so for tid from 0..23, h_=0, i=tid), then they write to addresses: `i*D + h_*hd + d`. For fixed h_ and d, this is `i*96 + constant`. Since 96 is a multiple of 32 (bank size 4 bytes, 32 banks), `i*96` maps to bank `(i*96/4) % 32 = (i*24) % 32`. For i from 0..23, this cycles through banks. 24 and 32 are not coprime (gcd=8). So addresses for i and i+8 will map to same bank? Let's compute: bank index = (i * 24) % 32. i=0 -> 0 i=1 -> 24 i=2 -> 48 % 32 = 16 i=3 -> 72 % 32 = 8 i=4 -> 96 % 32 = 0 i=5 -> 120 % 32 = 24 i=6 -> 144 % 32 = 16 i=7 -> 168 % 32 = 8 i=8 -> 192 % 32 = 0 So there are only 4 distinct banks used! Each bank gets 6 threads (24/4=6). So 6-way bank conflict per warp when writing smem_ctx. That's not great but maybe acceptable for such a small kernel. We can avoid bank conflicts by writing with a stride that is not a multiple of 32. For example, using `smem_ctx[h_ * N * hd + i * hd + d]` (the original (H,N,hd) layout). In that layout, consecutive i differ by hd=24. Address = h_*N*hd + i*hd + d. Bank = (i*24 + const) % 32. Same issue! Because hd=24, same as D? Wait, N*hd = 24*24 = 576. Address for fixed h_, varying i: `i*hd + const`. hd=24. Same bank pattern: (i*24) % 32. So same 6-way conflict. To avoid bank conflicts, we can transpose the storage: `smem_ctx[d * H * N + h_ * N + i]`. Then consecutive i differ by 1, which is perfect (no bank conflict). But then out_proj reads `smem_ctx[n*D + d_in]` which would not be contiguous. Another option: use `float` accesses with stride that causes bank conflicts only at 4-way or less. But 6-way is not terrible. The memory operation is small (24 writes per thread). So it might not matter. In the out_proj phase, reading `smem_ctx[n*D + d_in]` with consecutive `d_in` across threads? Wait, in out_proj, threads are parallelized over (n, d_out). For fixed n and d_out, threads iterate over d_in. No cross-thread shared memory access. So no bank conflict there. The bank conflict only happens in the write to smem_ctx in Phase 4. Since the kernel is memory-bandwidth bound but not on shared memory, and the total shared memory operations are tiny (4*24*24*24 writes ~ 55k writes), bank conflicts are negligible. So we can just write `smem_ctx[i*D + h_*hd + d]` for simplicity. Alternatively, we could use a different thread mapping for Phase 4: assign threads to (n,d) and have them accumulate over h and j? No. Let's just go with the straightforward indexing. Wait, there is also a read bank conflict in Phase 3: `smem_q[((h_*N)+i)*hd + d]` and `smem_k[((h_*N)+j)*hd + d]`. For a fixed linear, one thread reads q for varying d (sequential) and k for varying d (sequential). No cross-thread conflict. Phase 5b reads `smem_logits[base + j]` sequentially: no cross-thread conflict. Reads `smem_v[((h_*N)+j)*hd + d]`: for fixed (h_,i,d), threads in a warp handling the same h_,i but different d? No, each thread handles one (h_,i) and loops over d. So no cross-thread conflict. Therefore, the only cross-thread shared memory accesses that might conflict are the writes in Phase 4 (and Phase 1,2,3 writes are to different addresses? So consecutive threads write to consecutive banks -> no conflict. Phase 2: `smem_rel_bias[(h_*N+i)*N + j]`. For consecutive tid, p increments by 1, so (i,j) increments by 1. Address increments by 1 float -> no conflict. Phase 1: `smem_q[linear]` linear increments by 1 -> no conflict. So Phase 4 write is the only potential conflict. Let's calculate the conflict: `i*D + h_*hd + d`. For a warp handling linear = 0..31 (some have h=0, some h=1). The addresses are: For h=0: `i*96 + d`. For h=1: `i*96 + 24 + d`. For h=2: `i*96 + 48 + d`. For h=3: `i*96 + 72 + d`. The bank index for address `a` (in floats) is `(a) % 32` (since bank size = 4 bytes = 1 float). Actually bank = (byte_offset / 4) % 32. Since each float is 4 bytes, bank = `a % 32`. So for h=0: `(i*96 + d) % 32 = (i*0 + d) % 32` since 96 % 32 = 0. So bank = `d % 32`. For h=1: `(i*96 + 24 + d) % 32 = (24 + d) % 32`. For h=2: `(48 + d) % 32`. For h=3: `(72 + d) % 32`. Now, for a fixed d, threads with different (h_,i) but same d might write to the same bank if the offsets line up. For example, thread A: h=0,i=0,d=0 -> bank 0. Thread B: h=1,i=0,d=8 -> bank (24+8)%32 = 0. Are A and B in the same warp? Thread A has linear=0 (tid=0). Thread B has linear= (1*N + 0) = 24 (tid=24). Tid 0 and 24 are in different warps (warp 0: 0-31, warp 0 includes both!). Yes, tid 0 and 24 are in the same warp. They both write to bank 0 (for d=0 and d=8 respectively). But they write to different addresses (address 0 vs 24). Wait, bank 0 can service only one address per cycle, leading to serialization. So 2-way conflict in this case. Actually there might be many such collisions. Let's enumerate for warp 0 (tid 0..31): linear = tid (since tid < total_q=96 and step is 256, so first iteration linear=tid). For tid 0..23: h=0, i=tid. For tid 24..31: h=1, i=tid-24. For a given d, addresses for h=0,i are `i*96 + d`. Bank = `(i*96 + d) % 32 = d % 32`. So all 24 threads in h=0 write to bank `d % 32`! That means each write takes 24 cycles. That is indeed terrible. Wait, but the d-loop is inside the thread. For each thread, it writes 24 values (d=0..23). The pattern is not contiguous, but it's predictable. Actually, in out_proj, each thread loops over d_in and performs a multiply-accumulate. The reads are sequential by d_in. Whether the addresses are contiguous or not doesn't matter for latency if they fit in L1. Shared memory has 32 banks, but for a single thread, reads are pipelined. Bank conflicts only matter for simultaneous accesses across a warp. So using `smem_ctx[d * H * N + h_ * N + n]` eliminates write bank conflicts completely. Let's adopt this. Let's define: `#define SMEM_CTX(d, h, i) smem_ctx[(d) * H * N + (h) * N + (i)]` In Phase 4: `smem_ctx[d * H * N + h_ * N + i] = acc;` In Phase 5 (out_proj): ``` for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { int h_ = d_in / hd; int d = d_in % hd; float val = smem_ctx[d * H * N + h_ * N + n]; sum += val * out_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; } ``` Wait, is `smem_ctx` defined as `float* smem_ctx = smem_edge_mask + N * N;`? Wait, `d` ranges from 0 to hd-1 (since hd = D/H). So total elements = hd * H * N = D * N. Yes, same size as N*D. So `smem_ctx` has N*D floats. So different h_ map to different banks. For a given h_, consecutive i differ by 1 -> consecutive banks. So within a warp, if threads handle different i and same h_,d, there is no bank conflict. Now check Phase 5 read: each thread reads `smem_ctx[d * H * N + h_ * N + n]`. For a fixed thread, it iterates over d_in (which maps to h_ and d). The address changes by varying d and h_. Strided reads are fine. However, we might want to improve locality by reordering the loops. The out_proj is `y[d_out] = sum_{d_in} ctx[d_in] * W[d_out, d_in]`. Now, let's write the corrected kernel. **Refactoring the kernel:** We should be careful with the `hidden` array in Phase 2. If we declare `float hidden[16];` and index with `po`, compiler may put it in local memory. To avoid that, we could compute the MLP outputs directly without storing the hidden vector. But the second linear needs all P hidden values. We could compute the first linear, SiLU, and accumulate into the second linear output incrementally? No, because the second linear is matrix-vector multiply: `out[h] = sum_p hidden[p] * w2[h,p]`. If we compute hidden[p] one by one, we can accumulate: ```cuda for (int h_ = 0; h_ < H; ++h_) { float acc = rel_b2[h_]; for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) { // compute hidden[po] float s = rel_b1[po]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) { s += pair_feats[feat_base + pi] * rel_w1[po * P + pi]; } float x = s; float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); float hidden_val = x * sig; acc += hidden_val * rel_w2[h_ * P + po]; } smem_rel_bias[(h_ * N + i) * N + j] = acc; } ``` This computes the first linear and SiLU on the fly for each `po`, then uses it immediately. This avoids storing `hidden[16]`. However, it recomputes the first linear for each `h_`. Since H=4, it does the first linear 4 times per pair. The first linear is P*P=256 mul-adds. Doing it 4 times = 1024 ops per pair. Original approach with storage does 256 + 4*64 = 512 ops. So this is 2x more compute. But pairs are few (576) and ops are tiny. Let's see: 576 pairs * 1024 ops = 589k ops. That's negligible. But it avoids the local array. However, it also reads `pair_feats[feat_base + pi]` multiple times (4 times per pair). That's 4*16=64 reads per pair. Original with local array does 16 reads per pair. Given pair_feats is in global memory, reading it 4 times may increase memory traffic. But L1 cache should keep it. Actually, a better approach: load `pair_feats[feat_base + pi]` into registers first: ```cuda float pf[16]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) pf[pi] = pair_feats[feat_base + pi]; ``` Then compute hidden[po] using `pf`. This loads pair_feats once (16 floats). Then compute second linear using hidden. This requires storing hidden[16] (or recompute). If we store hidden, we have the array. If we compute on the fly for each h_, we still need to recompute hidden[po]. But we can compute hidden once and store in a local array. Let's just use the array. 16 floats local is fine. Let's try to be gentle on the compiler: we will use `#pragma unroll` with a compile-time constant? But P is dynamic. However, the compiler might not unroll. If P is 16, and we use a local array `float h[16];`, the compiler will likely allocate 16 registers or spill to local memory. Let's check if local memory usage is a concern. Local memory is cached in L1. With 256 threads, 16 floats/thread = 16KB local memory per block. L1 is 192KB per SM. So it's fine. Even if spilled, it won't hurt much. Actually, the simplest way to avoid any potential issue is to just declare the array and use it. The compiler usually handles small arrays fine. Let's write the code. One more consideration: `nodes` and `out` are `float*`. We should ensure that we use `__restrict__` for better optimization. Also, we can mark shared memory pointers as `__restrict__` though not necessary. Let's write the complete Python code. We'll create `ModelNew` with the parameters (using nn.Linear etc.) and the forward method calling the loaded CUDA function. We'll use `load_inline` at module load time. To avoid recompiling on every import, we can use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` which caches based on source code. The `cpp_sources` will contain the host function. The `cuda_sources` will contain the kernel. `functions = ['gf_vla_forward']`. Let's draft: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline _cuda_src = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> extern "C" __global__ void gf_vla_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ q_weight, const float* __restrict__ k_weight, const float* __restrict__ v_weight, const float* __restrict__ rel_w1, const float* __restrict__ rel_b1, const float* __restrict__ rel_w2, const float* __restrict__ rel_b2, const float* __restrict__ out_weight, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, float scale) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int hd = D / H; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_nodes_norm = smem; float* smem_q = smem_nodes_norm + N * D; float* smem_k = smem_q + N * D; float* smem_v = smem_k + N * D; float* smem_rel_bias = smem_v + N * D; float* smem_logits = smem_rel_bias + H * N * N; float* smem_edge_mask = smem_logits + H * N * N; float* smem_ctx = smem_edge_mask + N * N; // Load edge mask for (int p = tid; p < N * N; p += blockDim.x) { smem_edge_mask[p] = edge_mask[b * N * N + p]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm if (tid < N) { int n = tid; int base = ((b * N) + n) * D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { sum += nodes[base + d]; } float mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float diff = nodes[base + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float val = (nodes[base + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d]; smem_nodes_norm[n * D + d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); // Projections Q, K, V int total_nd = N * D; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_nd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; const float* x_ptr = smem_nodes_norm + n * D; float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = x_ptr[d_in]; sum_q += x * q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; sum_k += x * k_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; sum_v += x * v_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; } smem_q[linear] = sum_q; smem_k[linear] = sum_k; smem_v[linear] = sum_v; } __syncthreads(); // Pairwise MLP int total_pairs = N * N; for (int p = tid; p < total_pairs; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; int feat_base = ((b * N + i) * N + j) * P; float hidden[16]; for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) { float s = rel_b1[po]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) { s += pair_feats[feat_base + pi] * rel_w1[po * P + pi]; } hidden[po] = s; } for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) { float x = hidden[po]; float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); hidden[po] = x * sig; } for (int h_ = 0; h_ < H; ++h_) { float s = rel_b2[h_]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) { s += hidden[pi] * rel_w2[h_ * P + pi]; } smem_rel_bias[(h_ * N + i) * N + j] = s; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute logits int total_qk = H * N * N; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_qk; linear += blockDim.x) { int h_ = linear / (N * N); int rem = linear % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; float dot = 0.0f; int q_base = ((h_ * N) + i) * hd; int k_base = ((h_ * N) + j) * hd; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_q[q_base + d] * smem_k[k_base + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel_bias[linear]; if (smem_edge_mask[i * N + j] < 0.5f) { logit = -INFINITY; } smem_logits[linear] = logit; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax and attention * V int total_q = H * N; for (int linear = tid; linear < total_q; linear += blockDim.x) { int h_ = linear / N; int i = linear % N; int base = ((h_ * N) + i) * N; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float val = smem_logits[base + j]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_logits[base + j] - max_val); smem_logits[base + j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_logits[base + j] * inv_sum * smem_v[((h_ * N) + j) * hd + d]; } // Store in transposed layout to avoid bank conflicts later smem_ctx[d * H * N + h_ * N + i] = acc; } } __syncthreads(); // Out proj + residual for (int linear = tid; linear < total_nd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { int h_ = d_in / hd; int d = d_in % hd; float val = smem_ctx[d * H * N + h_ * N + n]; sum += val * out_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; } int global_idx = ((b * N) + n) * D + d_out; out[global_idx] = nodes[global_idx] + sum; } } torch::Tensor gf_vla_forward( torch::Tensor nodes, torch::Tensor pair_feats, torch::Tensor edge_mask, torch::Tensor norm_weight, torch::Tensor norm_bias, torch::Tensor q_weight, torch::Tensor k_weight, torch::Tensor v_weight, torch::Tensor rel_w1, torch::Tensor rel_b1, torch::Tensor rel_w2, torch::Tensor rel_b2, torch::Tensor out_weight) { nodes = nodes.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); pair_feats = pair_feats.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); edge_mask = edge_mask.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32); int B = nodes.size(0); int N = nodes.size(1); int D = nodes.size(2); int P = pair_feats.size(3); int H = rel_b2.size(0); float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(D / H)); auto out = torch::empty_like(nodes); size_t smem_size = (5 * N * D + 2 * H * N * N + N * N) * sizeof(float); auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); gf_vla_forward_kernel<<<B, 256, smem_size, stream>>>( nodes.data_ptr<float>(), pair_feats.data_ptr<float>(), edge_mask.data_ptr<float>(), norm_weight.data_ptr<float>(), norm_bias.data_ptr<float>(), q_weight.data_ptr<float>(), k_weight.data_ptr<float>(), v_weight.data_ptr<float>(), rel_w1.data_ptr<float>(), rel_b1.data_ptr<float>(), rel_w2.data_ptr<float>(), rel_b2.data_ptr<float>(), out_weight.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, D, P, H, scale); return out; } ''' ``` Wait, `load_inline` expects `cpp_sources` (C++ host code) and `cuda_sources` (CUDA device code) separately. If we put the kernel in `cuda_sources`, the host function in `cpp_sources` needs to know about the kernel (declared as `extern "C"`). reasoning
The compile is failing because `gf_vla_forward` isn't visible to the C++ side when it's only defined in the .cu file. With `load_inline`, when `cpp_sources` is empty or just headers, the function declaration needs to be in the C++ source. Let me try including the function declaration in `cpp_sources` and using `extern` properly. Actually, the issue is that the pybind wrapper is generated in `main.cpp` and it needs to see the declaration. A common setup is to have the function declaration in cpp_sources with `#include <torch/extension.h>` and the definition in cuda_sources. Since both get compiled and linked together, if we put the declaration in cpp_sources and definition in cuda_sources, it should work. Let me try: - cpp_sources: `#include <torch/extension.h>\ntorch::Tensor gf_vla_forward(...);` - This worked in my second attempt but gave a linking error CXXABI. Actually wait, in the second attempt, it compiled main.o successfully but then hit the CXXABI error. In the third attempt (cpp_sources="") it failed at main compilation because no declaration. So the second attempt was actually the correct approach, but it hit a runtime CXXABI error when importing the shared library. The CXXABI error is a known issue with `load_inline` when the GCC version used to compile the extension doesn't match the one used for PyTorch, or because of incompatible libstdc++. A fix can be to add `extra_cflags` or `extra_ldflags` to statically link libstdc++, or set environment variables like `LD_PRELOAD=/path/to/libstdc++.so.6`. Actually, I can't modify environment variables easily here. A common fix is to add `extra_cflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0']` or `extra_cflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=1']`, or more effectively, to add `extra_cuda_cflags` and `extra_cflags` with `-static-libstdc++`. Let's try adding `extra_cflags=['-static-libstdc++']` and `extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '-static-libstdc++']`. Also for the CXXABI issue, sometimes setting `_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0` helps. Actually, a well-known workaround for `load_inline` in KernelBench is to use `extra_cflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0', '-static-libstdc++']` and `extra_cuda_cflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0', '-O3']`. Let me try that with the correct structure (declaration in cpp, definition in cuda).

Kernel pseudo-code: __global__ void gf_vla_forward_kernel_v2(..., int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, float scale) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int hd = D / H; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: // smem_nodes_norm: N * D // smem_Q: N * hd // smem_K: N * hd // smem_V: N * hd // smem_rel: N * N // smem_logits: N (one row, reused) // smem_edge_mask: N * N // smem_ctx_row: hd (output for one i) float* smem_nodes_norm = smem; float* smem_Q = smem_nodes_norm + N * D; float* smem_K = smem_Q + N * hd; float* smem_V = smem_K + N * hd; float* smem_rel = smem_V + N * hd; float* smem_logits = smem_rel + N * N; float* smem_edge_mask = smem_logits + N; float* smem_ctx_row = smem_edge_mask + N * N; // 1. Compute LayerNorm for batch b into smem_nodes_norm // Each thread handles one or more n (rows) for (int n = tid; n < N; n += blockDim.x) { int base = ((b * N) + n) * D; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { sum += nodes[base + d]; } float mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float diff = nodes[base + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float val = (nodes[base + d] - mean) * inv_std; val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d]; smem_nodes_norm[n * D + d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); // 2. Q,K,V projections for head h // Each thread handles elements in smem_Q/K/V // Q[n, d] = sum_din smem_nodes_norm[n, din] * q_weight[h*hd + d, din] // smem_nodes_norm layout: [N, D] // weights layout: [D, D] (transposed in memory?) // In PyTorch Linear: y = x @ W^T. W shape is [D_out, D_in]. Data in memory: row-major by rows of W. // In our Python code we passed q_weight which is a 2D tensor of shape [D, D]. // If we access q_weight[d_out * D + d_in], that's row d_out, column d_in. // So dot product is correct. for (int linear = tid; linear < N * hd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / hd; int d = linear % hd; int d_out = h * hd + d; const float* x_ptr = smem_nodes_norm + n * D; float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = x_ptr[d_in]; sum_q += x * q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]; n sum_k += x * k_weight[d_out * D + d_in];\n sum_v += x * v_weight[d_out * D + d_in];\n }\n smem_Q[linear] = sum_q;\n smem_K[linear] = sum_k;\n smem_V[linear] = sum_v;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // 3. Compute pairwise rel bias into smem_rel\n // rel_mlp: MLP on pair_feats shape [B, N, N, P] -> [B, N, N, H]\n // For head h and batch b, compute one value per (i,j)\n int total_pairs = N * N;\n for (int p = tid; p < total_pairs; p += blockDim.x) {\n int i = p / N;\n int j = p % N;\n int feat_base = ((b * N + i) * N + j) * P;\n float hidden[16];\n for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) {\n float s = rel_b1[po];\n for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) {\n s += pair_feats[feat_base + pi] * rel_w1[po * P + pi];\n }\n hidden[po] = s;\n }\n for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) {\n float x = hidden[po];\n float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x));\n hidden[po] = x * sig;\n }\n float s = rel_b2[h];\n for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) {\n s += hidden[pi] * rel_w2[h * P + pi];\n }\n smem_rel[p] = s;\n }\n // Load edge_mask\n for (int p = tid; p < total_pairs; p += blockDim.x) {\n smem_edge_mask[p] = edge_mask[b * N * N + p];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // 4. Attention per row i\n // Output needs to be written to global memory: ctx[b, n, h, d]\n // We can write to out directly: out = nodes + out_proj(ctx)\n // That makes it harder to accumulate in shared memory because out_proj mixes all heads.\n // So we need to store ctx in global or shared memory. Since ctx for one head is N*hd = 576 floats, we can keep it in shared memory.\n // Actually we can keep ctx for all heads in global memory, or we can accumulate the out_proj contribution directly.\n // For simplicity, let's allocate smem_ctx for this head: N * hd floats.\n // Add another smem buffer: smem_ctx = smem_ctx_row + hd? Wait, we used smem_ctx_row for one row.\n // We can reuse smem_rel? No, we need rel for computing all logits. Then ctx is the output of the attention (before out_proj and residual). So `out = nodes + self.out_proj(ctx)` is correct. Let's refine the kernel. Potential issue: The kernel might be slow because global writes to ctx are not coalesced. For a given block (b, h), we write to `ctx[b, i, h*hd + d]`. Threads in a warp processing the same `i` but different `d` will write to contiguous memory (since d varies fastest). That's coalesced. If threads process different `i`, writes are strided by D. But within a warp, if all threads have the same `i`? Let's see: in the attention phase, I had `for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x)`. This means threads in a warp might have different `i` values. For example, if tid differs by 1, i differs by 1. Then the writes for `d=0` are at `base + i*D + h*hd`. Threads in a warp write to memory separated by D=96 floats = 384 bytes. This is not fully coalesced but might be okay with L1 cache for such small sizes. We can instead assign threads to process elements within a row. For N=24 and blockDim=256, we have 10 threads per row on average. It's better to parallelize over d within a row. Better approach for the attention loop: For each `i` from 0 to N-1: - All threads compute dot products for logits_j in parallel. Each thread handles one or more j. - Store logits in smem_logits. - Do softmax (reductions). Since N=24, a reduction across 24 elements in shared memory is fast. We can have one warp do it or all threads. - Then compute output d: each thread handles one or more d in 0..hd-1. This is more parallel and better coalesced. Let's code the attention for a fixed i as: ```cpp for (int i = 0; i < N; ++i) { // Step 1: compute logits for all j for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { float dot = 0.0f; int base_q = i * hd; int base_k = j * hd; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_Q[base_q + d] * smem_K[base_k + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel[i * N + j]; if (smem_edge_mask[i * N + j] < 0.5f) logit = -INFINITY; smem_logits[j] = logit; } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: softmax (parallel reduction for max and sum) // For N=24, we can just have one thread compute max and sum, or do a tree reduction. // With 256 threads, it's wasteful to have all do it, but fine. if (tid == 0) { // or tid < N float max_val = -INFINITY; n for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) max_val = fmaxf(max_val, smem_logits[j]);\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {\n float e = expf(smem_logits[j] - max_val);\n smem_logits[j] = e;\n sum += e;\n }\n smem_logits[N] = 1.0f / sum; // store inv_sum in an extra slot\n }\n __syncthreads();\n float inv_sum = smem_logits[N];\n // Step 3: compute ctx[i, d] for each d\n for (int d = tid; d < hd; d += blockDim.x) {\n float acc = 0.0f;\n for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {\n acc += smem_logits[j] * inv_sum * smem_V[j * hd + d];\n }\n ctx[((b * N) + i) * D + h * hd + d] = acc;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n } ``` This uses `smem_logits` of size N+1 = 25 floats. This is clean and decent. Wait, in step 2, using only tid==0 is okay for N=24. For larger N maybe not, but here it's fine. Total shared memory: - smem_nodes_norm: N*D = 2304 - smem_Q: N*hd = 576 - smem_K: 576 - smem_V: 576 - smem_rel: N*N = 576 - smem_edge_mask: N*N = 576 - smem_logits: N+1 = 25 Total = 2304 + 576*4 + 576*2 + 25 = 2304 + 2304 + 1152 + 25 = 5785 floats. ~23KB. Great. Now, there is a question about edge_mask layout. In the reference: `logits = logits.masked_fill(edge_mask.unsqueeze(1) < 0.5, float("-inf"))` edge_mask is shape (B, N, N). unsqueeze(1) makes it (B, 1, N, N), which is broadcast to match logits (B, H, N, N). So for each head, the same edge_mask is used. Thus in the kernel, using `smem_edge_mask[i*N + j]` is correct. Also, in the reference, `rel_bias` is shape (B, H, N, N), computed by permute(0,3,1,2) on rel_mlp output which is (B, N, N, H). So `rel_bias[b, h, i, j]` corresponds to `rel_mlp(pair_feats)[b, i, j, h]`. Our kernel computes for head h and batch b: `smem_rel[i*N + j]` correctly. One more thing: The reference code computes: `q = self.q_proj(h).view(B, N, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2)` Then `logits = matmul(q, k.transpose(-2,-1))` which is standard attention: [B, H, N, hd] @ [B, H, hd, N] -> [B, H, N, N]. Then `attn = softmax(logits, dim=-1)` Then `ctx = matmul(attn, v).transpose(1,2).reshape(B, N, D)` which gives ctx shape [B, N, D] where the head dimension is concatenated. In our kernel, for a fixed (b, h): - smem_Q shape [N, hd] where row i is q[b, h, i, :]. - smem_K shape [N, hd] where row j is k[b, h, j, :]. - dot = sum_d Q[i,d] * K[j,d]. - logit = dot * scale + rel_bias[b, h, i, j]. This is correct. Then `attn` is row-wise softmax over j. Then `ctx_i_d = sum_j attn[i,j] * V[j,d]`. Then we write `ctx[b, i, h*hd + d]`. Correct. Then Python `self.out_proj(ctx)` does exactly what `self.out_proj(ctx)` in the reference does. So the logic is numerically equivalent. Potential floating-point differences: - The reference uses PyTorch's softmax which is numerically stable. - Our kernel computes max and then sum of exp and divides. This is also numerically stable. - Differences may arise from order of operations. Since all operations are associative in different ways, there may be small mismatches within tolerance (1e-4). One concern: We use `float` (fp32) throughout. Good. Let's write the final code. I need to be careful with `nodes.data_ptr<float>()` etc. All tensors must be contiguous.
