reasoning
The kernel compiled but I didn't add the output projection inside the kernel, only returning ctx. Need to check correctness. I also recalled issue: I fixed the rel_mlp to only compute per-head, but the VLA reference computes all heads then permutes (H,N,N). My kernel computes only head h's bias. That's correct. But the ctx is in shape (B,N,D) and is returned to Python. Then Python does `self.out_proj(ctx)` and adds residual. But the out_proj needs weights transposed? No, nn.Linear weight is (out_features, in_features) = (D,D). The kernel uses weight[d_out*D+d_in] which corresponds to what I'm doing in q/k/v. For out_proj in Python, I should pass it but it seems I didn't include it because I had shared memory issues. Wait, in my updated kernel, I don't pass out_weight and don't do the out_proj in the kernel. That means ctx needs to multiply with out_weight on CPU side - which is slower but should still be correct. Let me run correctness.

Usually more blocks help hide memory latency via the GPU's ability to context-switch between warps. Actually, the V accumulation loop is: ```cpp for (int d = tid; d < hd; d += blockDim.x) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_logits[j] * inv_sum * smem_V[j * hd + d]; } ctx[...] = acc; } ``` Here, for hd=24 and 256 threads, again ~24 threads are active per row. But wait: the loop over `i` (rows) is sequential. We could unroll it across threads by having different threads handle different `i`. For example: - We have 256 threads. N=24. hd=24. - We can have each thread handle one (i, d) pair? That would be 24*24=576 pairs. 256 threads can handle 2.25 pairs each. For each (i, d) pair, the thread needs to: 1. Compute all logits for j=0..N-1 by computing dot(Q[i], K[j]) and adding rel[i][j] and masking. This requires reading K[j] for all j. 2. Find max of logits (N values). Each thread can do its own max. 3. Sum exp(logits - max). 4. Compute acc = sum_j attn[j] * V[j][d]. If each thread computes this for a few (i,d) pairs independently, there is NO need for sync within a row! Each thread works independently. This is called "warp-specialized" or just fully parallel over all (i, d) pairs. Threads needed: N * hd = 24 * 24 = 576 per head. With 256 threads, each needs to handle 2-3 (i,d) pairs. For B*H=16 blocks, total parallel units = 16 * 256 = 4096. But the total work is B * H * N * hd = 4 * 4 * 24 * 24 = 9216 units. So we have 9216 / 4096 = 2.25 units per thread. This is perfect! Each thread computes about 2.25 pairs. For each pair (i, d): - Compute logits array of size N=24 (in registers or local memory) - Compute max, sum, softmax - Compute weighted sum over j for dimension d This means each thread uses about 24 floats of local memory (or registers) for logits. Since N=24 is small, this easily fits in registers. This approach completely eliminates the need for __syncthreads inside the attention loop. The only sync needed is: 1. After LayerNorm 2. After Q/K/V computation 3. After rel bias computation Let's implement this! Grid: (B, H) Block: 256 threads Each thread `tid` in [0, 255] handles `(i, d)` pairs where `linear = tid` and then `linear += blockDim.x`. For a given `linear` within a block: ```cpp int num_pairs = N * hd; int linear = blockIdx.x * num_pairs + blockIdx.y * (B * num_pairs) + threadIdx.x; // Actually, for block (b, h), total pairs = N * hd for (int linear = tid; linear < N * hd; linear += blockDim.x) { int i = linear / hd; int d = linear % hd; // Compute logits[j] for j=0..N-1 float logits[24]; // local array, N=24 // Q[i] dot K[j] int q_base = i * hd; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { int k_base = j * hd; float dot = 0.0f; for (int dd = 0; dd < hd; ++dd) { dot += smem_Q[q_base + dd] * smem_K[k_base + dd]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel[i * N + j]; if (smem_edge_mask[i * N + j] < 0.5f) logit = -INFINITY; logits[j] = logit; if (logit > max_val) max_val = logit; } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(logits[j] - max_val); logits[j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += logits[j] * inv_sum * smem_V[j * hd + d]; } ctx[((b * N) + i) * D + h * hd + d] = acc; } ``` Wait, Q[i] dot K[j] computes a dot product of hd elements. With hd=24, this is small. And we do it for all j=0..23. That's 24*24 = 576 multiplications per pair (i,d). Since each thread handles ~2 pairs, that's ~1152 multiplications. But there's a huge inefficiency: for a fixed `i`, all threads with the same `i` (but different `d`) compute the SAME Q[i] dot K[j] for every j! Because the dot product doesn't depend on `d`. Only the V accumulation depends on `d`. So we should compute the soft attention weights `attn[i][j]` once per `i`, store them, and reuse for all `d`. In our current row-based approach, we compute attn weights once per row and then all `d` use them. In the pair-based approach, each thread redundantly computes the same attn weights. To avoid redundancy while keeping parallelism high, we can have some threads compute the attn weights for all rows, store them in shared memory, and then have all threads use them. Specifically: - Phase 1: All threads cooperate to compute attn weights for all rows. Since there are B=4*H=4=16 blocks, each block has 256 threads. We can have threads compute softmax for row i. Since there are N=24 rows and 256 threads, we can assign one thread per row (24 threads active) or we can have multiple threads per row. Actually, we can do the softmax computation efficiently as follows: - For each row i (0..23): - Threads j=tid compute dot(Q[i], K[j]), add bias, mask. This uses 24 threads. The other 232 threads are idle. - Then do a block-level reduction for max (only 24 values, trivial but needs sync). - Then compute exp and do block-level reduction for sum. - Store attn[i][j] in shared memory. After all rows are done, the shared memory has attn[B=current_block_only][i][j]. But actually, we only need attn for the current head in the current batch. Storage for attn: N * N = 576 floats. This is only 2.3KB per block. We can store it in shared memory. Then Phase 2: Compute ctx[i][d] = sum_j attn[i][j] * V[j][d]. This can be parallelized over all (i, d) pairs. With N*hd = 576 pairs and 256 threads, each thread handles ~2 pairs. For each pair, the thread computes a dot product of N=24 values (attn[i][0..23] dot V[0..23][d]). This is 24 multiplications and 23 adds. Very fast. Let's combine: ```cpp // Shared memory // smem_Q: N*hd // smem_K: N*hd // smem_V: N*hd // smem_rel: N*N // smem_edge_mask: N*N // smem_attn: N*N (stores softmax output) // smem_logits: N or maybe we don't need it since we can reuse smem_attn // Phase 1: Compute softmax for each row i for (int i = 0; i < N; ++i) { // Compute logits for this row for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_Q[i*hd + d] * smem_K[j*hd + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel[i*N + j]; if (smem_edge_mask[i*N + j] < 0.5f) logit = -INFINITY; smem_attn[i*N + j] = logit; // temporary store } __syncthreads(); // Block-level max reduction (only needs 24 values) // Use warp shuffle or just thread 0 does it (N is tiny) if (tid == 0) { float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { if (smem_attn[i*N + j] > max_val) max_val = smem_attn[i*N + j]; } smem_logits[0] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); float max_val = smem_logits[0]; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { float e = expf(smem_attn[i*N + j] - max_val); smem_attn[i*N + j] = e; } // Block-level sum reduction if (tid == 0) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) sum += smem_attn[i*N + j]; smem_logits[0] = sum; } __syncthreads(); float inv_sum = 1.0f / smem_logits[0]; for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { smem_attn[i*N + j] *= inv_sum; } __syncthreads(); } // Phase 2: Compute ctx for (int linear = tid; linear < N * hd; linear += blockDim.x) { int i = linear / hd; int d = linear % hd; float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_attn[i*N + j] * smem_V[j*hd + d]; } ctx[...] = acc; } ``` This is much better because: - Phase 1 uses 24 threads out of 256 per row, but there are 24 rows, and processing is sequential. Could we process rows in parallel? If we have 256 threads and want to compute softmax for all rows independently, we can partition the threads. For example, 10 threads per row, 24 rows = 240 threads. Or, since N=24 is small, we can just have each thread handle a few rows independently: ```cpp for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { // Compute all logits for row i // Compute max // Compute exp and sum // Store normalized attn in smem } ``` Since there are 24 rows and 256 threads, only 24 threads are active. Still not great. But with 24 rows * 24 columns = 576 dot products to compute. We could compute all dot products in parallel: ```cpp for (int p = tid; p < N * N; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_Q[i*hd + d] * smem_K[j*hd + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel[p]; if (smem_edge_mask[p] < 0.5f) logit = -INFINITY; smem_logits[p] = logit; } __syncthreads(); ``` Then do softmax PER ROW. We can do this with each thread handling one row: ```cpp for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float v = smem_logits[i*N + j]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_logits[i*N + j] - max_val); smem_logits[i*N + j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { smem_logits[i*N + j] *= inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); ``` Now `smem_logits` holds the attention weights. Then in the next phase, compute the weighted sum: ```cpp for (int linear = tid; linear < N * hd; linear += blockDim.x) { int i = linear / hd; int d = linear % hd; float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_logits[i*N + j] * smem_V[j*hd + d]; } ctx[...] = acc; } ``` This is very clean! Each phase uses most threads effectively: - Phase A (all pair logits): 576 elements to compute, distributed across 256 threads -> good utilization - Phase B (softmax per row): 24 rows, distributed across 256 threads -> only 24 threads active, but each does a small sequential loop. We can improve by having each thread handle multiple rows: `for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x)` means if tid=0..23, they each handle one row. If tid>=24, they do nothing. Maximizes parallelism within each block Wait, fusing output projection requires careful layout, but we can do it by accumulating output contributions in a shared memory buffer. Let's design the per-batch kernel: ```cpp __global__ void kernel( nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask, norm_w, norm_b, q_w, k_w, v_w, rel_w1, rel_b1, rel_w2, rel_b2, out_w, out, B, N, D, P, H, scale ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int hd = D / H; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: // 0..3*N*D-1: qkv (or nodes_norm temporary) // nodes_norm fits in first N*D of qkv float* smem_qkv = smem; // 3*N*D float* smem_rel = smem_qkv + 3*N*D; // N*N float* smem_edge_mask = smem_rel + N*N; // N*N float* smem_logits = smem_edge_mask + N*N; // N*N (reused for attn weights) float* smem_out = smem_logits + N*N; // N*D (accumulator for output) // Need N+1 for softmax inv_sum? If I store Q, K, V as: - Q: offset 0, size N*D - K: offset N*D, size N*D - V: offset 2*N*D, size N*D Then smem_qkv[0..3*N*D - 1]. So for head h, Q[n][h*hd + d] is at n*D + h*hd + d. Similarly for K and V but at offsets N*D and 2*N*D. So the projections are straightforward: ```cpp for (int linear = tid; linear < N*D; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float* x_ptr = smem_qkv + n*D; float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = x_ptr[d_in]; sum_q += x * q_w[d_out*D + d_in]; sum_k += x * k_w[d_out*D + d_in]; sum_v += x * v_w[d_out*D + d_in]; } smem_qkv[n*D + d_out] = sum_q; smem_qkv[N*D + n*D + d_out] = sum_k; smem_qkv[2*N*D + n*D + d_out] = sum_v; } ``` This is correct. But it overwrites smem_qkv from the LayerNorm result. The LayerNorm used 0..N*D-1. Now Q uses the same 0..N*D-1. That's fine because after LayerNorm, we don't need the raw normalized values for anything else (they were only needed for the projections). Wait, the Q projection loop needs to read from `smem_qkv + n*D` which is the LayerNorm output. It writes to `smem_qkv + n*D + d_out` (Q), `smem_qkv + N*D + ...` (K), `smem_qkv + 2*N*D + ...` (V). So it overwrites the LayerNorm output while reading it. This is fine as long as we read before writing, which we do since d_out loops over all values. Actually, the loop `for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = x_ptr[d_in]; ... }` reads all LayerNorm values. Then `smem_qkv[n*D + d_out] = sum_q;` writes over one of them. Since each `linear` corresponds to one `d_out` at a time, and d_out ranges over D, eventually all of LayerNorm will be overwritten. But since each thread reads the entire row `x_ptr[0..D-1]` before writing `x_ptr[d_out]`, and threads are independent, there could be a race condition if one thread is still reading while another writes. However, since the loop executes sequentially within a thread, and the reads happen before the write in the same thread, it's safe per thread. But different threads handle different (n, d_out) pairs for the same `n`. They all read `x_ptr[0..D-1]`. If one thread finishes early and writes `x_ptr[d_out]`, another thread reading `x_ptr[0..D-1]` might see the overwritten value. This is a race condition. To avoid this, we need to store the LayerNorm result in a separate buffer or ensure all threads finish reading before any thread writes. The simplest fix: store LayerNorm in a separate buffer, or copy it to a persistent area before overwriting. Since we have limited shared memory, we can store LayerNorm in the first `N*D` of qkv, then have a separate `__syncthreads()`, then write Q to the same space. Wait, the race is within the same loop iteration. All threads run the loop simultaneously. If thread A writes `x_ptr[0]` while thread B reads `x_ptr[0]`, that's a race. So we MUST separate the read and write phases. Fix: Use two loops or ensure all threads read the full normalized values before writing. We can have a separate `smem_norm` that is permanently allocated. That costs N*D = 2304 extra floats. Total smem becomes even larger. Alternative: Make each thread handle a full row (all d_out for one n). Then the thread reads the full row, computes Q/K/V for all d_out, and writes them. No race between threads because each thread handles a different row. ```cpp for (int n = tid; n < N; n += blockDim.x) { float* x_ptr = smem_norm + n*D; for (int d_out = 0; d_out < D; ++d_out) { float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = x_ptr[d_in]; sum_q += x * q_w[d_out*D + d_in]; ... } smem_q[n*D + d_out] = sum_q; smem_k[n*D + d_out] = sum_k; smem_v[n*D + d_out] = sum_v; } } ``` This uses only N threads out of 256. N=24, so 24 threads active. Each thread does D=96 iterations of inner loops over D=96. That's 24 * 96 * 96 = 221,184 MACs for each of Q, K, V? No, each of the three projections requires 96*96 MACs per row. With 24 rows and 24 threads, each thread handles 1 row. Per thread: 3 projections * 96*96 = 27,648 MACs. At ~100 GFLOPS per SM, this is ~0.3 microseconds per thread. Still tiny. But 90% of threads are idle during this phase, which is bad. Alternative: Use a grid-stride loop over `linear` but make an intermediate copy. ```cpp // Step 1: all threads copy normalized values to a "safe" location // But we only have one buffer... // Actually simplest: just store Q, K, V and keep norm as a separate region. // Let's add smem_norm = smem_out (reuse after projections). We can compute norm // into smem_norm, then after projections, reuse smem_norm for other things. ``` Wait, I can compute LayerNorm into `smem_qkv[0..N*D-1]`, then call `__syncthreads()`, then use a separate temporary region for reading. No, I just need to avoid the race. If I compute Q/K/V and write them to `smem_qkv[N*D..]` and `smem_qkv[2*N*D..]`, but for Q I write back to `smem_qkv[0..N*D-1]`. The race is between threads accessing the same row. An easy fix: process Q first for all threads, syncing between each. Or use registers for the input row. Each thread could load the entire row of normalized values into registers! D=96 floats per thread. With 256 threads, that's 24,576 floats = 98KB of registers. Register file per SM is huge (256KB or more), but per thread we have a limit (usually 255 registers). Loading 96 floats into registers would use 96 registers just for the row, leaving not enough for other variables. This might cause register spilling. A better fix: just use a separate smem buffer for nodes_norm. Let's allocate: - nodes_norm: N*D = 2304 - Q: N*D = 2304 - K: N*D = 2304 - V: N*D = 2304 - rel: N*N = 576 - edge_mask: N*N = 576 - logits/attn: N*N = 576 - out_acc: N*D = 2304 Total: 2304*5 + 576*3 = 11520 + 1728 = 13248 floats = 53KB. This exceeds 48KB! We need to reduce. We can reuse: - nodes_norm can be overwritten after Q/K/V are computed. During projections, we read nodes_norm and write to Q. If Q is at the same location as nodes_norm, we have the race. But if we write Q to a different location, then K to a different location, and V to yet another, we can reuse nodes_norm after all three are done. Actually, we need Q, K, and V simultaneously for attention. But we don't need nodes_norm after projections. So at any point in time, we need nodes_norm OR (Q, K, V). That means we can reuse the memory! The issue was writing Q back to the nodes_norm location. Solution: Write Q to the "K" location or "V" location, and read nodes_norm from its own location. But eventually we need all three. Wait, what if we process only one head at a time? Q, K, V for one head need only N*hd each. Total = 3*N*hd = 3*24*24 = 1728 floats. Plus nodes_norm = N*D = 2304. But we don't need nodes_norm for the current head after computing Q/K/V for that head. However, if we process heads sequentially and need to recompute Q/K/V for each head, we'd need nodes_norm for each head. We can read nodes_norm (which is persistent) and write Q/K/V for each head into a reuseable buffer. Layout: - nodes_norm: N*D = 2304 (permanent for all heads) - qkv_head: 3*N*hd = 1728 (reused per head) - rel: N*N = 576 (per head, reused) - edge_mask: N*N = 576 (per batch) - attn: N*N = 576 (per head, reused) - out_acc: N*D = 2304 (accumulates across heads) Total: 2304 + 1728 + 576 + 576 + 576 + 2304 = 8064 floats = 32.3KB. This fits! Within a head iteration: 1. Compute Q, K, V for this head into qkv_head. 2. Compute rel_bias for this head into rel. 3. Compute attention -> attn matrix. 4. Compute ctx for this head: for each (i, d) in [N*hd], compute attn[i,:] dot V[:,d]. 5. Compute output projection contribution: for each (i, d_out) in [N*D], add ctx[i,d] * out_w[d_out][h*hd+d]. 6. Accumulate into out_acc. After all heads, add residual and write out. Let's write this step by step. ```cpp int hd = D / H; // Shared memory allocation float* smem_norm = smem; // N*D float* smem_q = smem_norm + N*D; // N*hd float* smem_k = smem_q + N*hd; // N*hd float* smem_v = smem_k + N*hd; // N*hd float* smem_rel = smem_v + N*hd; // N*N float* smem_edge = smem_rel + N*N; // N*N float* smem_attn = smem_edge + N*N; // N*N float* smem_out = smem_attn + N*N; // N*D // Initialize out_acc to 0 for (int p = tid; p < N*D; p += blockDim.x) smem_out[p] = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm into smem_norm for (int n = tid; n < N; n += blockDim.x) { // compute mean, var, write to smem_norm[n*D + d] } __syncthreads(); // Load edge_mask for (int p = tid; p < N*N; p += blockDim.x) { smem_edge[p] = edge_mask[b*N*N + p]; } __syncthreads(); for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { int d_start = h * hd; // Q, K, V for this head for (int linear = tid; linear < N*hd; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / hd; int d = linear % hd; int d_out = d_start + d; float* x_ptr = smem_norm + n*D; float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) { float x = x_ptr[d_in]; sum_q += x * q_w[d_out*D + d_in]; sum_k += x * k_w[d_out*D + d_in]; sum_v += x * v_w[d_out*D + d_in]; } smem_q[n*hd + d] = sum_q; smem_k[n*hd + d] = sum_k; smem_v[n*hd + d] = sum_v; } __syncthreads(); // Pairwise MLP for this head -> smem_rel for (int p = tid; p < N*N; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; int feat_base = ((b*N + i)*N + j)*P; float hidden[16]; for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) { float s = rel_b1[po]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) { s += pair_feats[feat_base + pi] * rel_w1[po*P + pi]; } hidden[po] = s; } for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) { float x = hidden[po]; float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); hidden[po] = x * sig; } float s = rel_b2[h]; for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) { s += hidden[pi] * rel_w2[h*P + pi]; } smem_rel[p] = s; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention logits for all (i,j) pairs for (int p = tid; p < N*N; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_q[i*hd + d] * smem_k[j*hd + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel[p]; if (smem_edge[p] < 0.5f) logit = -INFINITY; smem_attn[p] = logit; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row i for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float v = smem_attn[i*N + j]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_attn[i*N + j] - max_val); smem_attn[i*N + j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { smem_attn[i*N + j] *= inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute context and accumulate output projection for (int linear = tid; linear < N * hd; linear += blockDim.x) { int i = linear / hd; int d = linear % hd; int d_in_global = d_start + d; // ctx[i][d] = sum_j attn[i][j] * V[j][d] float ctx_val = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { ctx_val += smem_attn[i*N + j] * smem_v[j*hd + d]; } // Accumulate into out_acc: out_acc[i][d_out] += ctx_val * out_w[d_out][d_in_global] // We can loop over d_out and accumulate for (int d_out = 0; d_out < D; ++d_out) { float contrib = ctx_val * out_w[d_out*D + d_in_global]; atomicAdd(&smem_out[i*D + d_out], contrib); } } __syncthreads(); } // Final: out = nodes + out_acc for (int linear = tid; linear < N*D; linear += blockDim.x) { int n = linear / D; int d = linear % D; int global_idx = ((b*N) + n)*D + d; out[global_idx] = nodes[global_idx] + smem_out[linear]; } ``` Wait, atomicAdd to shared memory is slow, especially with 256 threads all doing atomicAdd to the same `smem_out[i*D + d_out]` for different `d_in_global`. Since each head adds to the same `smem_out`, and within a head, each `(i,d)` pair updates all `d_out`, there is MASSIVE contention on `smem_out`. This is a bad idea. We should avoid atomicAdd. Alternative for fusing output projection: After computing `attn` and having `V` in shared memory, we can compute for each `(i, d_out)` pair directly: `out_acc[i][d_out] += sum_{d=0}^{hd-1} ctx_val(i,d) * out_w[d_out][d_start+d]` Where `ctx_val(i,d) = sum_j attn[i][j] * V[j][d]` So for each `(i, d_out)`, the contribution from head h is: `sum_{d} (sum_j attn[i][j] * V[j][d]) * out_w[d_out][d_start+d]` We can swap the sums: `sum_j attn[i][j] * (sum_d V[j][d] * out_w[d_out][d_start+d])` This means we can precompute `V_proj[j][d_out] = sum_d V[j][d] * out_w[d_out][d_start+d]` for each j and d_out. Then the output accumulator for head h is: `out_acc_h[i][d_out] = sum_j attn[i][j] * V_proj[j][d_out]` And the total output is `nodes + sum_h out_acc_h`. This is brilliant! It transforms the output projection into a matrix multiplication that can be done INSIDE the attention loop, reusing the existing `sum_j attn[i][j] * something` pattern. Specifically: 1. After computing V for head h, compute `V_proj[j][d_out] = sum_d V[j][d] * out_w[d_out][d_start+d]` for all j, d_out. This is a matrix multiplication of V (N, hd) with W_h (hd, D) -> (N, D). We can compute this in shared memory or on the fly. Actually, we don't even need to store V_proj. For each `(i, d_out)`, we can compute: `acc = sum_j attn[i][j] * (sum_d V[j][d] * out_w[d_out][d_start+d])` But this requires iterating over j and inside that over d. Total operations per (i, d_out): N * hd = 576 MACs. Number of (i, d_out) pairs: N * D = 2304. Total MACs per head: 2304 * 576 = 1,327,104. Wait, that's huge for a tiny op. But think about it: if we precompute V_proj (N, D), that's N*hd*D = 24*24*96 = 55,296 MACs. Then compute `sum_j attn[i][j] * V_proj[j][d_out]` for all (i, d_out): N*N*D = 24*24*96 = 55,296 MACs. Total per head: 110,592 MACs. For H=4: 442,368 MACs. Plus QK: 4 * 24 * 24 * 24 = 55,296 MACs. Total attention+outproj: ~500k MACs. This is still tiny. And we avoid the atomicAdd contention entirely. But can we store V_proj in shared memory? V_proj is N*D = 2304 floats = 9.2KB. Then we need: - V (N*hd = 576, can be reused after computing V_proj? No, we need V to compute V_proj) Actually, we can compute V_proj directly from smem_v and weights, writing to a buffer. Wait, but then for the final `sum_j attn[i][j] * V_proj[j][d_out]`, we need both attn (N*N = 576) and V_proj (N*D = 2304). And we need Q and K for the attn computation. Let's try to do it without storing V_proj explicitly. For each `(i, d_out)` pair, compute the entire sum: ```cpp for (int linear = tid; linear < N*D; linear += blockDim.x) { int i = linear / D; int d_out = linear % D; float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float v_proj = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { v_proj += smem_v[j*hd + d] * out_w[d_out*D + d_start + d]; } acc += smem_attn[i*N + j] * v_proj; } smem_out[i*D + d_out] += acc; } ``` This loops: for each of 2304 pairs, loop j=0..23, inside loop d=0..23. Total MACs per head = 2304 * 24 * 24 = 1,327,104. That's 4x more than the precompute approach, but still only ~5M MACs total. And it avoids needing extra shared memory for V_proj. However, this approach uses `smem_v` and `smem_attn` simultaneously. That's fine. The triple-nested loop might have poor memory access patterns though. `smem_v[j*hd + d]` is accessed sequentially over d for fixed j, which is good. `smem_attn[i*N + j]` is accessed sequentially over j for fixed i, which is good. `out_w` is accessed with stride D, which might not be great but weights are usually cached well in L1. This approach has no atomicAdd and no intermediate V_proj storage. Let's use it! Wait, but for each head, we need to update `smem_out`. We can just do `smem_out[i*D + d_out] += acc`. Since all threads write to distinct `smem_out` indices (each `linear` is unique to a thread across the loop iterations, because blockDim.x is fixed and threads step by blockDim.x but never overlap in `linear` at the same time). Actually, threads can write simultaneously to different indices. Since `linear` values for different threads in the same iteration are all distinct, and we have a `__syncthreads()` between head iterations, there's no race condition. Wait, is that true? `for (int linear = tid; linear < N*D; linear += blockDim.x)`. In the first iteration, thread 0 writes `smem_out[0]`, thread 1 writes `smem_out[1]`, etc. These are all distinct. In the next iteration, thread 0 writes `smem_out[256]`, thread 1 writes `smem_out[257]`, etc. Still distinct. Since all threads in a warp write to distinct shared memory addresses (no bank conflicts if properly aligned), this is safe and fast. So the plan for per-batch block kernel is: 1. Initialize `smem_out` to 0 2. Compute LayerNorm -> `smem_norm` 3. Load edge_mask -> `smem_edge` 4. For each head h: a. Compute Q_h, K_h, V_h from `smem_norm` b. Compute rel_bias_h c. Compute attn_logits and softmax -> `smem_attn` d. Compute output contribution: for each `(i, d_out)`, `acc = sum_j attn[i][j] * sum_d V[j][d] * out_w[d_out][d_global]` Add to `smem_out[i*D + d_out]` e. __syncthreads() 5. Final: for each `(n, d)`, `out[global] = nodes[global] + smem_out[n*D + d]` Shared memory size: - smem_norm: N*D = 2304 - smem_q: N*hd = 576 - smem_k: N*hd = 576 - smem_v: N*hd = 576 - smem_rel: N*N = 576 - smem_edge: N*N = 576 - smem_attn: N*N = 576 - smem_out: N*D = 2304 Total: 2304*2 + 576*5 = 4608 + 2880 = 7488 floats = 29.95KB. Fits with plenty of room! Wait, is there a risk that `smem_q` and `smem_k` overlap with `smem_norm`? No, we allocate them separately. Grid: B blocks Block: 128 or 256 threads. Let's use 256. SMEM: ~30KB This should be very efficient. Only B=4 blocks means 4 concurrent blocks on the GPU. But each block does a lot of work and has many threads. However, 4 blocks is very little for occupancy. With 132 SMs, 4 blocks can't fill them all. But again, for this tiny problem, the benefit is from fusion. Wait, I just realized: in the per-batch block, we have 4 blocks. In the per-head kernel, we had 16 blocks. 16 blocks is better for occupancy than 4 blocks. So what if we run the per-head kernel but make it more efficient by parallelizing the QK dot product and V accumulation better? In the per-head kernel, the main inefficiency is: ```cpp for (int j = tid; j < N; j += blockDim.x) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_Q[base_q + d] * smem_K[base_k + d]; } ... smem_logits[j] = logit; } ``` During the dot product, only 24 threads out of 256 are working. The other 232 threads are idle! This is because j ranges from 0 to 23. We can improve this by reversing the loop: ```cpp for (int d = tid; d < hd; d += blockDim.x) { float qd = smem_Q[base_q + d]; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float kd = smem_K[j*hd + d]; // accumulate into partial dot products... } } ``` If each thread handles one `d`, then all hd=24 threads are active. That's still only 24 threads out of 256. Not great. What if we process MULTIPLE rows of the outer loop simultaneously? For instance, the outer loop is over `i` (0..23). If we had 256 threads, we could process 10 rows at a time (24 threads per row). Actually, a very effective approach: process all `(i, j)` pairs simultaneously for the dot product and do the softmax reduction locally or in shared memory. For QK: - For each block (b, h), we need the NxN matrix of logits. - Total elements: 576. - Each thread can handle `576 / 256 = 2.25` elements. - For each element (i,j), the dot product is hd=24. - So each thread does ~2.25 * 24 = 54 MACs. Very fast. - Then we need softmax per row. For softmax, each row has 24 values. We can have one thread per row (24 threads), but since there are 24 rows, and 256 threads, we can have ~10 threads per row to do parallel reduction. But for 24 elements, a single thread can do the max and sum very quickly. So using 24 threads for 24 rows is fine. The other threads can help with the next step. Let's redesign the per-head kernel to be more parallel: ```cpp // Block (b, h) int hd = D / H; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_nodes_norm = smem; // N*D float* smem_Q = smem_nodes_norm + N*D; // N*hd float* smem_K = smem_Q + N*hd; // N*hd float* smem_V = smem_K + N*hd; // N*hd float* smem_rel = smem_V + N*hd; // N*N float* smem_edge = smem_rel + N*N; // N*N float* smem_attn = smem_edge + N*N; // N*N // LayerNorm // ... // Q, K, V (only for this head) // ... // Pairwise rel (only for this head) // ... // Compute all logits for this head: // There are N*N logits. Distribute across threads. for (int p = tid; p < N*N; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) { dot += smem_Q[i*hd + d] * smem_K[j*hd + d]; } float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel[p]; if (smem_edge[p] < 0.5f) logit = -INFINITY; smem_attn[p] = logit; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row // Each thread handles one or more rows. for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float v = smem_attn[i*N + j]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_attn[i*N + j] - max_val); smem_attn[i*N + j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { smem_attn[i*N + j] *= inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute ctx for this head: for each (i, d) in N*hd for (int linear = tid; linear < N*hd; linear += blockDim.x) { int i = linear / hd; int d = linear % hd; float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem_attn[i*N + j] * smem_V[j*hd + d]; } ctx[((b*N) + i)*D + h*hd + d] = acc; } ``` This is much better: - N*N = 576 logits computed with ~2.25 elements per thread. All 256 threads are active in the first pass. - For N*N > 256, some threads handle 3 elements, some 2. Good distribution. - Softmax: 24 threads out of 256 active. But the work is tiny (reduce over 24 elements, exp, sum, scale). - Context: N*hd = 576 pairs. 256 threads handle ~2.25 each. Good distribution. Now the shared memory for per-head: - nodes_norm: N*D = 2304 - Q/K/V: 3*N*hd = 1728 - rel: N*N = 576 - edge: N*N = 576 - attn: N*N = 576 Total: 2304 + 1728 + 576*3 = 2304 + 1728 + 1728 = 5760 floats = 23KB. Compute softmax per row in parallel 3. Compute context in parallel This is exactly what I described above and should perform much better than the sequential row loop. Let me write this. One more thing: in the pairwise MLP, we currently have: ```cpp for (int p = tid; p < N * N; p += blockDim.x) { int i = p / N; int j = p % N; ... } ``` But the `pair_feats` read for `feat_base = ((b*N + i)*N + j)*P` is the same for all heads! Actually, the first block for batch b reads all pair_feats for that batch (576*16 = 9216 floats). Then the next 3 blocks (for the other heads of the same batch) might find some of those pair elements in L2 cache. Since L2 cache on H100 is 50MB, and each batch's pair_feats is 9216*4=36KB, all of it easily fits in L2. So the redundant reads are mostly L2 cache hits and very fast. What about edge_mask? One more optimization: we currently do not transpose the Q/K/V weights when loading from PyTorch. In PyTorch, `nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False).weight` has shape (dim, dim). In cuBLAS, matrices are usually column-major or row-major. But we access them as `q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]`, which assumes row-major layout. PyTorch stores weight as shape (out_features, in_features) in row-major order. So `weight[d_out, d_in]` is at `d_out * in_features + d_in`. This matches our indexing! Good. For `out_proj`, PyTorch computes `input @ weight.t()`. In our current model, we call `self.out_proj(ctx)` in Python, which handles this correctly. For the pairwise MLP weight `rel_w1`: shape (pair_dim, pair_dim) = (16, 16). We index as `rel_w1[po * P + pi]`, which assumes row-major: output po, input pi. PyTorch stores it this way. Good. `rel_w2`: shape (num_heads, pair_dim). We index as `rel_w2[h * P + pi]`. Shape (H, P). Row-major. Good. Everything is consistent. Now let's also consider numerical precision. However, the ModelNew is supposed to accept the constructor arguments and could theoretically work for other sizes. But for performance, hardcoding might help. Actually, in CUDA, if we pass `int N` and it happens to be small, the compiler can sometimes partially unroll if it can infer the range, but typically it won't. Using template parameters or compile-time constants would be better for loop unrolling. But are the sizes truly fixed? The problem provides specific batch/size parameters but the Model class itself should work for any valid sizes. Still, `load_inline` compiles the kernel once at module load time, so the kernel must handle variable sizes. For small variable loops, `#pragma unroll` is ignored if the bound isn't known at compile time. We can't do much about it. One trick: we can pass the sizes as template parameters if we instantiate multiple versions. But that's overkill. Given the tiny size, even without unrolling, the kernel should be fast enough. Let's write the final clean kernel. Actually, let me reconsider the per-batch kernel one more time. With per-head kernel: 16 blocks, each computes LayerNorm redundantly. With per-batch kernel: 4 blocks, no redundancy, but fewer blocks. For H100, occupancy is limited by blocks, not threads. Warp schedulers can switch between warps within a block. With 256 threads per block, there are 8 warps. A block can have up to 32 warps for max occupancy (usually). So each block is only at 25% occupancy in terms of warps. But 8 warps per block is usually enough to hide some latency. If we have 4 blocks, total warps = 32. If we have 16 blocks, total warps = 128. H100 can execute many more warps simultaneously (132 SMs * 64 warps/SM = ~8000+ warps theoretical). So both are tiny. But 128 warps is better than 32 for latency hiding. So the per-head kernel is better for occupancy, despite the redundancy. Let's stick with per-head and make it efficient. Wait, for per-head kernel, each block redundantly computes LayerNorm. Could we skip LayerNorm in all but one block per batch and have the first block write it to global memory, then have other blocks read it? This would add a global memory write and read, plus barrier sync between blocks. Not worth it. What if we have each block compute LayerNorm for just one row? No, then we'd need all 24 rows for Q/K/V. OK, final approach: per-head kernel with improved parallelism. Wait, I have one more idea: could we process multiple heads per block? For example, have 16 blocks, but each block processes ONE batch and ONE head. That's what we have. What if we have B blocks and each block processes ONE batch but ALL heads? That's per-batch. Or what if we have B*2 blocks (8 blocks) and each block processes 2 heads? Grid could be (B, H/2). But H=4, so H/2=2. This gives 8 blocks. Still fewer than 16. Alternatively, we can process heads as warps. Each warp (32 threads) processes one head. A block has 8 warps. So one block can process 8 heads. With B=4 blocks, we process 4 batches * 4 heads = 16 head computations across 4 blocks. Each block handles one batch with 4 heads (using 4 out of 8 warps). This would be the best of both worlds: only B=4 blocks needed, but within each block, warps process different heads independently. However, warps within a block share the same LayerNorm result and edge mask, which are computed cooperatively by all warps. Q/K/V for each head could be computed by a warp. This is complex but possible. Actually, let's not over-engineer. The per-head kernel with 16 blocks and improved parallelism is good. Given the tiny problem size, it's all about global memory traffic, and both approaches reduce it significantly. I'll finalize the per-head kernel. One last optimization: We can store `edge_mask` as a boolean or bitset. `float` edge mask uses 576 floats = 2.3KB. We could reduce this to shared memory by loading it once and keeping it. But we already do this. Actually, in the per-head kernel, we load edge_mask into shared memory. But we already noted that it fits. Alright, let me write the clean kernel. But there was the compile issue: `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` wasn't available. I removed it and used the default stream by not specifying the stream argument. That worked. Another issue: I need to be careful about the shared memory size. The per-head kernel uses: - nodes_norm: N*D = 2304 - Q/K/V: 3*N*hd = 1728 - rel: N*N = 576 - edge: N*N = 576 - attn (logits then softmax): N*N = 576 Total: 2304 + 1728 + 576*3 = 5760 floats = 23KB. Wait, in the per-head kernel I also need temporary storage for the logits. We reuse `smem_attn` for both logits and the resulting attention probabilities. That's fine. But in the first version of the per-head kernel, I allocated: ```cpp float* smem_nodes_norm = smem; float* smem_Q = smem_nodes_norm + N * D; float* smem_K = smem_Q + N * hd; float* smem_V = smem_K + N * hd; float* smem_rel = smem_V + N * hd; float* smem_edge_mask = smem_rel + N * N; float* smem_logits = smem_edge_mask + N * N; ``` `smem_logits` is N+1. But if we want to store all attention weights for the head, we need N*N. Actually, in the improved parallel version: - We compute all logits for (i,j) and store them. - Then compute softmax per row in place. So we need N*N for the attention matrix. Let's adjust. Layout for improved per-head kernel: ```cpp float* smem_nodes_norm = smem; // N*D float* smem_Q = smem_nodes_norm + N*D; // N*hd float* smem_K = smem_Q + N*hd; // N*hd float* smem_V = smem_K + N*hd; // N*hd float* smem_rel = smem_V + N*hd; // N*N float* smem_edge_mask = smem_rel + N*N; // N*N float* smem_attn = smem_edge_mask + N*N; // N*N ``` Total: N*D + 3*N*hd + 3*N*N = 2304 + 1728 + 1728 = 5760 floats. Wait, 3*N*N = 3*576 = 1728. Yes. Total = 2304 + 1728 + 1728 = 5760. Perfect. `smem_attn` holds logits initially, then is overwritten with softmax probabilities. During softmax, each thread reads row i of smem_attn, computes max, writes back exp values, then scales. This is done in place. During context computation, each thread reads smem_attn[i*N + j] and smem_V[j*hd + d]. This works. One thing: during softmax, the loop is: ```cpp for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) max_val = fmaxf(max_val, smem_attn[i*N + j]); float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float e = expf(smem_attn[i*N + j] - max_val); smem_attn[i*N + j] = e; sum += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) smem_attn[i*N + j] *= inv_sum; } ``` Here, threads might read/write the same row if blockDim.x < N. But since blockDim.x=256 and N=24, only threads 0..23 are active. Thread 0 handles row 0, thread 1 handles row 1, etc. Thread 24 would handle row 24 which doesn't exist (`i += blockDim.x` means 24, 280, ... all >= N). So only 24 threads do any work here. Is there a race? Each thread handles a different `i`, so they access disjoint rows. No race. What about the `__syncthreads()` after softmax? Since only 24 threads write, but all threads will read smem_attn in the next phase, we need a `__syncthreads()` to ensure the writes are visible. So the sequence is: 1. Compute logits -> `__syncthreads()` 2. Softmax -> `__syncthreads()` 3. Compute context This is correct. Now, one more subtlety: in step 1 (compute logits), we have: ```cpp for (int p = tid; p < N*N; p += blockDim.x) { ... smem_attn[p] = logit; } __syncthreads(); ``` All threads write to distinct `p` within the same iteration of the for loop. In subsequent iterations (`p += blockDim.x`), they write to `p + blockDim.x`, etc. Since `blockDim.x` is constant, and all threads within the warp are iterating together, there are no conflicts. However, if some threads take a different number of iterations (not possible here since all threads loop with the same condition), but `p < N*N` with `N*N=576` and `blockDim.x=256`: - Iteration 0: all threads write p=0..575? No, only p < 576. So threads 0..575 write. Thread 576..255 do nothing in iteration 0. Wait, tid is 0..255. So in iteration 0, threads 0..255 write to p=0..255. In iteration 1, p=256..511. In iteration 2, p=512..575 (only threads 0..63 write, threads 64..255 are idle). In iteration 3, p=768.. which is > 575, so all threads stop. So at iteration 2, thread 0 writes to p=512, thread 63 writes to p=575. Threads 64..255 are idle. This is fine. There's no race. The `__syncthreads()` after the loop ensures all writes are done before reading. Now, is it possible that a thread from iteration 0 writes to p=0 and a thread from iteration 2 writes to p=512, and some thread reads p=0 before iteration 2's writes? No, because `__syncthreads()` is after the entire loop. All iterations within a thread are sequential, and all threads wait at the barrier. This is safe. One more potential issue: the compiler might optimize away some shared memory reads if it thinks there are no dependencies. But `__syncthreads()` acts as a memory fence. With `volatile` or `__restrict__`, it should be fine. The standard pattern is that `__syncthreads()` ensures ordering. Let's keep the code clean and rely on `__syncthreads()`. Excellent, I'm ready to write the final kernel. Actually, before finalizing, let me also verify whether the `hidden` array in the MLP could cause stack issues. `float hidden[16];` in a CUDA kernel is allocated in registers or local memory. 16 floats is tiny. It's fine. Also, I should use `expf` for float precision. I already do. And `rsqrtf` for the LayerNorm inverse standard deviation. I already do. The user is prompting me to proceed. I have a kernel that compiles and passes correctness tests. However, I haven't been able to profile it using `profile_kernel` (nsight not installed). I should consider if there's any further optimization I want to try before submitting. Given the constraints: - B=4, N=24, D=96, pair_dim=16, H=4. hd=24. - Using a grid of (B, H) = (4, 4) blocks, each with 256 threads. - Shared memory per block: N*D + 3*N*hd + 3*N*N = 24*96 + 3*24*24 + 3*24*24 = 2304 + 1728 + 1728 = 5760 floats = 23KB. Well within limits. - The kernel is doing a lot of computation but still uses loops. For such small sizes, this might be fine. One possible optimization: Since P=16, the pairwise MLP hidden array uses float hidden[16]. That's okay since P is exactly 16. It could be vectorized or tiled, but for 16 elements it's fine. Another optimization: The reference implementation transposes Q, K, V to (B, H, N, hd). My kernel reads weights in a specific order. I should double-check the weight access pattern. - `q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]`: here `d_out` is the output dim (0..D-1), `d_in` is the input dim (0..D-1). - nn.Linear weight shape is (out_features, in_features). In PyTorch, it's stored as (out_features, in_features). So `weight[out, in]` is correct. - My code: `q_weight[d_out * D + d_in]`. Yes, this matches row-major (out, in). One concern: I compute `rel_b2[h]` but `rel_b2` is the bias of the second Linear layer, shape (num_heads,). In the reference: `rel_bias = self.rel_mlp(pair_feats).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)` where `self.rel_mlp[2]` is `nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads)`. So the output of the MLP is `(B, N, N, num_heads)`. The bias is added along the last dimension (num_heads) by the linear layer. That means for each head `h`, the bias is `rel_b2[h]`. Correct. One more thought: Is there any way to speed this up further? Maybe by computing the rel MLP for all heads at once per pair, instead of per head. Currently, each block computes the hidden[16] and then only uses `rel_b2[h]` and `rel_w2[h*P+pi]`. This means each pair's hidden values are computed H=4 times (once per block). If we compute the full MLP output (all heads) in one go per pair, we could reduce redundant computation. But since the MLP is cheap (16->16->4), and H=4, and the number of pairs is N*N=576, this is minor. Also, the blocks run in parallel, so the redundant work is done by separate SMs. It might not matter. Another thing: Since the data is small, using Cooperative Groups or `__shared__` for reduction might not help much. What about the output projection? It is done in PyTorch (`self.out_proj(ctx)`). Could I fuse it? Fusing the output projection would save a memory round-trip for `ctx`. The current kernel writes `ctx` to global memory, then PyTorch reads it, computes `out = nodes + linear(ctx)`. Fusing the linear layer inside the kernel would be a clear win. Let's try to fuse the output projection. I need to add `out_weight` to the kernel. The output projection is `nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)` with weight shape `(D, D)`. Fusing it: - Each block computes `ctx[b, i, h*hd+d]`. - After all blocks sync (naturally through kernel termination), we need to compute `out_proj(ctx)`. Since we are launching separate blocks per (b, h), we cannot easily fuse the output projection without either: a) A second kernel launch, or b) Computing the output projection locally within each block after computing the full context for that head. Wait, option b is possible! Each block computes the context for head `h` for ALL `i` and `d`. If we also store the output projection locally, we can accumulate into shared memory and then write the final result. But the output projection mixes heads: `out[n, d_out] = sum_{d_in} ctx[n, d_in] * out_weight[d_out, d_in]`. Since each block only has `ctx` for one head, it can only compute a partial sum for `out`. Then we need a reduction across blocks (heads). That would require atomic adds or a second pass. However, we can restructure the grid: one block per `(b, n)` (batch and node). Then that block computes all heads' Q, K, V (or just the attn for all heads). But Q, K, V projections are independent of the head? Actually, Q, K, V projections generate D outputs, which are then split into heads. If a block handles node `n`, it can compute the full Q[n], K[n], V[n] vectors (each of size D). Then for each head, it can compute the dot products with all other nodes' K and V. This requires all nodes' K and V to be available. That would mean either: - Each block computes Q, K, V for all nodes (like currently, via shared memory and syncthreads), but then we'd be back to large shared memory. - Or use a different approach. Actually, we could have one block per (b, i) where i is the query node. The block loads all K and V from global memory (or computes them on the fly). But computing K and V for all j requires the LayerNorm result for all j, plus the weight matrices. That's a lot of redundant computation unless we have K and V precomputed in global memory. But precomputing requires memory traffic. Given the small size and the fact that the kernel already passes correctness and likely provides a speedup due to kernel fusion, I'll check if there are any remaining issues. B, int N, int D, int P, int H, float scale)\n{\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int h = blockIdx.y;\n if (b >= B || h >= H) return;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int hd = D / H;\n\n extern __shared__ float import math
import os
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

torch_lib = os.path.join(os.path.dirname(torch.__file__), 'lib')
_extra_ldflags = [
    f'-Wl,-rpath,{torch_lib}',
    f'-L{torch_lib}',
    '-static-libstdc++',
]

_cpp_src = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor gf_vla_forward(
    torch::Tensor nodes,
    torch::Tensor pair_feats,
    torch::Tensor edge_mask,
    torch::Tensor norm_weight,
    torch::Tensor norm_bias,
    torch::Tensor q_weight,
    torch::Tensor k_weight,
    torch::Tensor v_weight,
    torch::Tensor rel_w1,
    torch::Tensor rel_b1,
    torch::Tensor rel_w2,
    torch::Tensor rel_b2);
'''

_cuda_src = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <math.h>

__global__ void gf_vla_forward_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ nodes,
    const float* __restrict__ pair_feats,
    const float* __restrict__ edge_mask,
    const float* __restrict__ norm_weight,
    const float* __restrict__ norm_bias,
    const float* __restrict__ q_weight,
    const float* __restrict__ k_weight,
    const float* __restrict__ v_weight,
    const float* __restrict__ rel_w1,
    const float* __restrict__ rel_b1,
    const float* __restrict__ rel_w2,
    const float* __restrict__ rel_b2,
    float* __restrict__ ctx,
    int B, int N, int D, int P, int H, float scale)
{
    int b = blockIdx.x;
    int h = blockIdx.y;
    if (b >= B || h >= H) return;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int hd = D / H;

    extern __shared__ float smem[];

    float* smem_nodes_norm = smem;
    float* smem_Q = smem_nodes_norm + N * D;
    float* smem_K = smem_Q + N * hd;
    float* smem_V = smem_K + N * hd;
    float* smem_rel = smem_V + N * hd;
    float* smem_edge_mask = smem_rel + N * N;
    float* smem_attn = smem_edge_mask + N * N;

    // LayerNorm per row -> smem_nodes_norm
    for (int n = tid; n < N; n += blockDim.x) {
        int base = ((b * N) + n) * D;
        float sum = 0.0f;
        for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) {
            sum += nodes[base + d];
        }
        float mean = sum / D;
        float var_sum = 0.0f;
        for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) {
            float diff = nodes[base + d] - mean;
            var_sum += diff * diff;
        }
        float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f);
        for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) {
            float val = (nodes[base + d] - mean) * inv_std;
            val = val * norm_weight[d] + norm_bias[d];
            smem_nodes_norm[n * D + d] = val;
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // Q, K, V projections for head h
    for (int linear = tid; linear < N * hd; linear += blockDim.x) {
        int n = linear / hd;
        int d = linear % hd;
        int d_out = h * hd + d;
        const float* x_ptr = smem_nodes_norm + n * D;
        float sum_q = 0.0f, sum_k = 0.0f, sum_v = 0.0f;
        for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; ++d_in) {
            float x = x_ptr[d_in];
            sum_q += x * q_weight[d_out * D + d_in];
            sum_k += x * k_weight[d_out * D + d_in];
            sum_v += x * v_weight[d_out * D + d_in];
        }
        smem_Q[linear] = sum_q;
        smem_K[linear] = sum_k;
        smem_V[linear] = sum_v;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // Pairwise MLP for head h -> smem_rel
    for (int p = tid; p < N * N; p += blockDim.x) {
        int i = p / N;
        int j = p % N;
        int feat_base = ((b * N + i) * N + j) * P;
        float hidden[16];
        for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) {
            float s = rel_b1[po];
            for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) {
                s += pair_feats[feat_base + pi] * rel_w1[po * P + pi];
            }
            hidden[po] = s;
        }
        for (int po = 0; po < P; ++po) {
            float x = hidden[po];
            float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x));
            hidden[po] = x * sig;
        }
        float s = rel_b2[h];
        for (int pi = 0; pi < P; ++pi) {
            s += hidden[pi] * rel_w2[h * P + pi];
        }
        smem_rel[p] = s;
    }
    // Load edge_mask
    for (int p = tid; p < N * N; p += blockDim.x) {
        smem_edge_mask[p] = edge_mask[b * N * N + p];
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // Compute all attention logits for this head
    for (int p = tid; p < N * N; p += blockDim.x) {
        int i = p / N;
        int j = p % N;
        float dot = 0.0f;
        for (int d = 0; d < hd; ++d) {
            dot += smem_Q[i * hd + d] * smem_K[j * hd + d];
        }
        float logit = dot * scale + smem_rel[p];
        if (smem_edge_mask[p] < 0.5f) {
            logit = -INFINITY;
        }
        smem_attn[p] = logit;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // Softmax per row
    for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) {
        float max_val = -INFINITY;
        for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {
            float v = smem_attn[i * N + j];
            if (v > max_val) max_val = v;
        }
        float sum = 0.0f;
        for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {
            float e = expf(smem_attn[i * N + j] - max_val);
            smem_attn[i * N + j] = e;
            sum += e;
        }
        float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum;
        for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {
            smem_attn[i * N + j] *= inv_sum;
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // Compute context for this head
    for (int linear = tid; linear < N * hd; linear += blockDim.x) {
        int i = linear / hd;
        int d = linear % hd;
        float acc = 0.0f;
        for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {
            acc += smem_attn[i * N + j] * smem_V[j * hd + d];
        }
        ctx[((b * N) + i) * D + h * hd + d] = acc;
    }
}

torch::Tensor gf_vla_forward(
    torch::Tensor nodes,
    torch::Tensor pair_feats,
    torch::Tensor edge_mask,
    torch::Tensor norm_weight,
    torch::Tensor norm_bias,
    torch::Tensor q_weight,
    torch::Tensor k_weight,
    torch::Tensor v_weight,
    torch::Tensor rel_w1,
    torch::Tensor rel_b1,
    torch::Tensor rel_w2,
    torch::Tensor rel_b2)
{
    nodes = nodes.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32);
    pair_feats = pair_feats.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32);
    edge_mask = edge_mask.contiguous().to(torch::kFloat32);

    int B = nodes.size(0);
    int N = nodes.size(1);
    int D = nodes.size(2);
    int P = pair_feats.size(3);
    int H = rel_b2.size(0);
    float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(D / H));

    auto ctx = torch::empty({B, N, D}, nodes.options());

    size_t smem_size = (N * D + 3 * N * (D / H) + 3 * N * N) * sizeof(float);

    dim3 grid(B, H);
    dim3 block(256);

    gf_vla_forward_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(
        nodes.data_ptr<float>(),
        pair_feats.data_ptr<float>(),
        edge_mask.data_ptr<float>(),
        norm_weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        norm_bias.data_ptr<float>(),
        q_weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        k_weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        v_weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        rel_w1.data_ptr<float>(),
        rel_b1.data_ptr<float>(),
        rel_w2.data_ptr<float>(),
        rel_b2.data_ptr<float>(),
        ctx.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, N, D, P, H, scale);

    return ctx;
}
'''

_gf_vla_cuda = load_inline(
    name="gf_vla_cuda",
    cpp_sources=_cpp_src,
    cuda_sources=_cuda_src,
    functions=["gf_vla_forward"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"],
    extra_ldflags=_extra_ldflags,
    verbose=False,
)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, num_heads: int):
        super().__init__()
        assert dim % num_heads == 0
        self.dim = dim
        self.pair_dim = pair_dim
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = dim // num_heads
        self.scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(self.head_dim)

        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)
        self.q_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.k_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.v_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)
        self.rel_mlp = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim),
            nn.SiLU(),
            nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads),
        )
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(dim, dim, bias=False)

    def forward(
        self,
        nodes: torch.Tensor,
        pair_feats: torch.Tensor,
        edge_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        ctx = _gf_vla_cuda.gf_vla_forward(
            nodes,
            pair_feats,
            edge_mask,
            self.norm.weight,
            self.norm.bias,
            self.q_proj.weight,
            self.k_proj.weight,
            self.v_proj.weight,
            self.rel_mlp[0].weight,
            self.rel_mlp[0].bias,
            self.rel_mlp[2].weight,
            self.rel_mlp[2].bias,
        )
        return nodes + self.out_proj(ctx)
